### Subjects

  * [Development studies](/subjects/development-studies)
  * [Science, technology and society](/subjects/science-technology-and-society)



## Abstract

The rapid advancement and deployment of Artificial Intelligence (AI) poses significant regulatory challenges for societies. While it has the potential to bring many benefits, the risks of commercial exploitation or unknown technological dangers have led many jurisdictions to seek a legal response before measurable harm occurs. However, the lack of technical capabilities to regulate this sector despite the urgency to do so resulted in regulatory inertia. Given the borderless nature of this issue, an internationally coordinated response is necessary. This article focuses on the theoretical framework being established in relation to the development of international law applicable to AI and the regulatory authority to create and monitor enforcement of said law. The authors argue that the road ahead remains full of obstacles that must be tackled before the above-mentioned elements see the light despite the attempts being made currently to that end.

Article Open access 12 April 2024

## Introduction

Artificial Intelligence (AI) presents a unique challenge for the international community, requiring a flexible approach (Gurkaynak et al., [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR135 "Gurkaynak G, Yilmaz I, Haksever G \(2016\) Stifling artificial intelligence: human perils. CLSR 32:749–758. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.clsr.2016.05.003
                  
                "), Abulibdeh et al., [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR2 "Abulibdeh A, Zaidan E, Abulibdeh R \(2024\) Navigating the confluence of artificial intelligence and education for sustainable development in the era of industry 4.0: Challenges, opportunities, and ethical dimensions. Journal of Cleaner Production, 140527")) as nations race for its development (Armstrong et al., [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR19 "Armstrong S, Bostrom N, Shulman C \(2016\) Racing to the precipice: a model of artificial intelligence development. AI Soc 31:201–206. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-015-0590-y
                  
                ")) despite the uncertainties it entails (Emery-Xu et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR95 "Emery-Xu N, Park A, Trager R \(2023\) Uncertainty, information, and risk in international technological races. J Confl Resol. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/00220027231214996
                  
                ")). Its rapid growth, the decision-making responsibilities given to it, and the significance of its outcomes lead regulators to seek its governance through human principles. These include human oversight, understanding, and ethical reasoning (Miailhe and Hodes, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR222 "Miailhe N, Hodes C \(2017\) The third age of artificial intelligence. Field Actions Sci Rep: Artif Intell Robot City 17:6–11")). The end goal is building trust and ensuring its effective governance (Alalawi et al., [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR6 "Alalawi Z et al. \(2024\) Trust AI regulation? Discerning users are vital to build trust and effective AI regulation. Artificial Intelligence. 1–18. 
                  https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2403.09510
                  
                ")). Laws are needed primarily because its progress is highly unpredictable and uncontrollable, and numerous risks have been identified (Buiten, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR41 "Buiten MC \(2019\) Towards intelligent regulation of artificial intelligence. Eur J Risk Regul 10\(1\):41–59. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/err.2019.8
                  
                ")). These include introducing AI systems capable of changing people’s behavior, exploiting vulnerabilities, and examining the trustworthiness of citizens (EU AI Act, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR364 "EU AI Act \(2024\) European Parliament legislative resolution of 13 March 2024 on the proposal for a regulation of the European Parliament and of the Council on laying down harmonised rules on Artificial Intelligence \(Artificial Intelligence Act\) and amending certain Union Legislative Acts \(COM\(2021\)0206 – C9-0146/2021 – 2021/0106\(COD\)\)")). However, a balance must be struck to ensure that rules do not impede progress. While national and local regulations are important, a global framework is better suited to address the limitations at the transnational level. Government legislation often focuses on the development of the local AI industry, its ethics, and challenges (Turner, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR304 "Turner J \(2019\) Robot rules: regulating artificial intelligence. Springer, Dordrecht")). Nonetheless, nations may be hesitant to address global governance and the need for rules and institutions. A universal regime could provide model laws, drawing on expertise from nations around the world. This would be a first step towards greater harmonization (DiMatteo et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR87 "DiMatteo LA, Poncibò C, Cannarsa M \(2022\) The Cambridge handbook of artificial intelligence: global perspectives on law and ethics. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")).

Nations and companies are investing heavily in this field. However, this poses a potential risk for developing and Least Developed Countries (LDCs), who may find themselves left behind (Miailhe and Hodes, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR222 "Miailhe N, Hodes C \(2017\) The third age of artificial intelligence. Field Actions Sci Rep: Artif Intell Robot City 17:6–11")). Governments with significant AI investments may view it as a state-controlled resource. Countries with limited technological development capacities may argue that it should be considered a common heritage of mankind. The regulation of this sector at the international level is complex due to competing interests. Some may prioritize innovation and economic growth over regulation, while others may argue for a more cautious approach. The problems identified require coordination via a new global AI regulatory authority to tackle current and future issues emerging in this field. The objective is to anticipate and respond to rapidly changing technological developments, balance the interests of different stakeholders, and provide the necessary technical expertise and resources to effectively regulate this sector. Doing so will help ensuring that the benefits are shared, and the risks associated with its development are minimized.

This article discusses the framework to be created globally based on which a regulatory authority is established with the mission of enacting and monitoring enforcement of international law considering the relevant stakeholders. The authors argue that eventually a new transnational legal architecture is bound to be created for AI similar to what happened with other topics and challenges. They will also highlight current attempts towards that direction showing that further efforts are needed to that end and that many factors and circumstances must be considered including the current state of international law and organizations, their effectiveness, and states participation.

The article starts with presenting the theoretical framework and then a literature review. After that, the role of the different actors in this context is examined (states; natural persons; civil society organizations; companies and international organizations). The role of the AI regulatory authority with establishing and monitoring international law is later discussed. Based on the above, the authors will set the road ahead for the regulation of this field.

## AI risks for the international community

The use of AI presents various risks that directly or indirectly impact the international community. These risks depend on the level of AI development and human agency (Turchin and Denkenberger, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR301 "Turchin A, Denkenberger D \(2020\) Classification of global catastrophic risks connected with artificial intelligence. AI Soc 35:147–163. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-018-0845-5
                  
                ")). They include potential job losses and social instability in sectors such as finance, healthcare, and entertainment; the use of AI as a weapon; lack of accountability and transparency; algorithmic bias; data privacy breaches; virtual threats and cyber conflicts (Taeihagh, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR291 "Taeihagh A \(2021\) Governance of artificial intelligence. Policy Soc 40:137–157. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/14494035.2021.1928377
                  
                "); Lin, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR198 "Lin TCW \(2019\) Artificial intelligence, finance, and the law. Fordham Law Rev 88:531–551. 
                  https://ir.lawnet.fordham.edu/flr/vol88/iss2/6
                  
                "); Butcher and Beridze, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR47 "Butcher JG, Beridze I \(2019\) What is the state of artificial intelligence governance globally? RUSI 164:88–96. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694260
                  
                ")). Other risks involve dependence on technology, loss of human connection, fairness, interpretability, safety, robustness and security, lawfulness, misinformation and manipulation, lack of compliance, and economic inequality and taxation (Gov.UK, [n.d.](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR382 "Gov.UK \(n.d.\) International scientific report on the safety ofadvanced AI. 
                  https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/international-scientific-report-on-the-safety-of-advanced-ai#:~:text=Future%20advances%20in%20general%2Dpurpose,could%20result%20in%20catastrophic%20outcomes
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); Marr, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR210 "Marr B \(2023\) The 15 biggest risks of artificial intelligence. Forbes. 
                  https://www.forbes.com/sites/bernardmarr/2023/06/02/the-15-biggest-risks-of-artificial-intelligence/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); Golbin et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR129 "Golbin I et al \(2020\) Responsible AI: a primer for the legal community. In Proceedings IEEE international conference on Big Data. 2121–2126"); Calo, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR50 "Calo R \(2017\) Artificial intelligence policy: a primer and roadmap. UC Davis Law Rev 51:399–435")). AI poses significant risks to peace and security, necessitating a coordinated response (Ovink, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR237 "Ovink C \(2024\) AI risks for international peace and security. Observer Research Foundation. 
                  https://www.orfonline.org/expert-speak/ai-risks-for-international-peace-and-security
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). It can globally impact hardware through viruses, damage populations via military autonomous systems, be used by governments to control citizens and lead to a war-inducing arms race. Critical global infrastructure, such as power generation, food supply, and transport systems, may be hacked, destroying technological advancements. Biohacking viruses, ransomware, and autonomous weapons are also concerns. Regarding human society, risks include AI’s impact on the market economy, potential human replacement by robots, brain modification technologies, a transnational totalitarian computer system, dangerous scientific progress, global health issues, and the potential for AI to control the planet. These risks can emerge at various stages of AI development (Turchin and Denkenberger, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR301 "Turchin A, Denkenberger D \(2020\) Classification of global catastrophic risks connected with artificial intelligence. AI Soc 35:147–163. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-018-0845-5
                  
                "); Schwalbe and Wahl, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR270 "Schwalbe N, Wahl B \(2020\) Artificial intelligence and the future of global health. Lancet 395:1579–1586. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/S0140-6736\(20\)30226-9
                  
                ")).

## AI benefits for the international community

The use of AI brings a wide range of benefits, including economic growth, improved performance, increased comfort and efficiency, enhanced safety through crime detection, and sustainability improvements. It also fosters social development and better quality outcomes in fields like healthcare and education (Sharma, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR274 "Sharma S \(2024\) Benefits or concerns of AI: a multistakeholder responsibility. Futures 157:1–10"); Alhosani and Alhashmi, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR8 "Alhosani K, Alhashmi SM \(2024\) Opportunities, challenges, and benefits of AI innovation in government services: A review. Discov Artif Intell 4\(8\):1–19"); AI for Good, n.d.). However, some argue that these benefits should not come at the expense of human rights (Marwala, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR214 "Marwala T \(2024\) We need good governance to shape AI for good. UNU. 
                  https://unu.edu/article/we-need-good-governance-shape-ai-good
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). There are calls for frameworks to guide AI development to benefit all humanity equally (Gibbons, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR125 "Gibbons ED \(2021\) Toward a more equal world: the human rights approach to extending the benefits of artificial intelligence. IEEE Technol Soc Mag 40\(1\):25–30. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1109/MTS.2021.3056295
                  
                ")), with global governance playing a crucial role in ensuring this (Leslie et al., [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR195 "Leslie D et al. \(2024\) ‘Frontier AI’, power, and the public interest: who benefits, who decides? Harvard Data Sci Rev. 1–20")) by prioritizing ethics, the rule of law, and addressing challenges (UN, [2023a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR311 "United Nations \(2023a\) Interim report: governing AI for humanity. Published by advisory body on artificial intelligence. Accessed 20 November 2024"), [2023b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR312 "United Nations \(2023b\) International community must urgently confront new reality of generative, artificial intelligence, speakers stress as security council debates risks, rewards. 
                  https://press.un.org/en/2023/sc15359.doc.htm
                  
                . 20 November 2024")). International groups, such as the AI Alliance, have been established to ensure the responsible use of technology for everyone’s benefit (IBM, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR161 "IBM \(2023\) AI alliance launches as an international community of leading technology developers, researchers, and adopters collaborating together to advance open, safe, responsible AI. 
                  https://newsroom.ibm.com/AI-Alliance-Launches-as-an-International-Community-of-Leading-Technology-Developers,-Researchers,-and-Adopters-Collaborating-Together-to-Advance-Open,-Safe,-Responsible-AI
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); US Department of State, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR365 "United States Department of State \(2023\) Artificial intelligence for accelerating progress on the sustainable development goals: Addressing society’s greatest challenges. 
                  https://www.state.gov/artificial-intelligence-for-accelerating-progress-on-the-sustainable-development-goals-addressing-societys-greatest-challenges/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). It’s worth noting that the benefits may vary between the North and South due to differing priorities and perspectives (Anthony et al., [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR9 "Anthony A, Sharma L, Noor E \(2024\) Advancing a more global agenda for trustworthy artificial intelligence. Carnegie Endowment for International Peace. 
                  https://carnegieendowment.org/research/2024/04/advancing-a-more-global-agenda-for-trustworthy-artificial-intelligence?lang=en
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). This is necessary because AI research is becoming increasingly complex and resource-intensive, with different approaches to innovation further complicating the situation (Kerry et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR172 "Kerry CF et al. \(2021\) Strengthening international cooperation on AI. 
                  https://www.brookings.edu/wp-content/uploads/2021/10/Strengthening-International-Cooperation-AI_Oct21.pdf
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). The AI advisory body of the UN, in its report “Governing AI for Humanity,” established the first guiding principle that AI should be governed inclusively, by and for the benefit of all. It justified this by noting that most of the global population is not capable of using AI to improve their lives, necessitating multi-stakeholder cooperation (AI Advisory Body, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR3 "AI Advisory Body \(2023\) Interim report: Governing AI for humanity. 
                  https://www.un.org/techenvoy/sites/www.un.org.techenvoy/files/ai_advisory_body_interim_report.pdf
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

## Why AI needs to be regulated internationally?

The risks posed by AI have the potential to create various social, economic, and ethical problems. Ignoring these risks could result in significant global negative effects and hinder technological progress. This is where international laws play a crucial role, setting goals and standards to reduce the likelihood of risks (Cha, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR56 "Cha S \(2024\) Towards an international regulatory framework for AI safety: lessons from the IAEA’s nuclear safety regulations. HSSC 11:1–13")), depending on their level and potential damage (Strous, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR284 "Strous L \(2019\) Should artificial intelligence be more regulated? In: Strous L, Cerf VG \(eds\) Internet of things. Information processing in an increasingly connected world. Springer, Dordrecht, p 28–34")), which can occur in any territory (Trager et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR299 "Trager T et al \(2023\) International governance of civilian AI: a jurisdictional certification approach. 1–51")). Stakeholders, including national and international policymakers, lawyers, industry experts, and academics, must be involved (Pesapane et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR232 "Pesapane F et al. \(2021\) Legal and regulatory framework for AI solutions in healthcare in EU, US, China, and Russia: new scenarios after a pandemic. Radiation 1:261–276. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3390/radiation1040022
                  
                ")). Key issues requiring regulation include human rights protection, questions of sovereignty, state responsibility, dispute settlement, and the North-South divide (Lee, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR191 "Lee J \(2022\) Artificial intelligence and international law. Springer, Dordrecht")). The ultimate goal is to balance societal interests with safe innovation while considering corporate interests (Cha, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR56 "Cha S \(2024\) Towards an international regulatory framework for AI safety: lessons from the IAEA’s nuclear safety regulations. HSSC 11:1–13")). Since the AI industry is global, with networks and computing resources spread across many countries, international cooperation is essential (Trager et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR299 "Trager T et al \(2023\) International governance of civilian AI: a jurisdictional certification approach. 1–51")). As AI entities are deemed to be operating within legal bounds as long as they are subject to human control, they should not be left to the discretion of states and corporations as they may prioritize their interests over other considerations (Burri, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR45 "Burri T \(2018\) International law and artificial intelligence. GYIL 60:91–108. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3790/gyil.60.1.91
                  
                ")). Moreover, confusion and uncertainty will rain if we use solutions on a case-by-case basis (Andrés, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR13 "Andrés MB \(2021\) Towards legal regulation of artificial intelligence. Rev IUS 15:35–53. 
                  https://doi.org/10.35487/rius.v15i48.2021.661
                  
                ")). As control issues become more prevalent, the accumulated experience of this field dealing with similar matters could provide valuable guidance (Boon, [2014](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR33 "Boon K \(2014\) Are control tests fit for the future? The slippage problem in attribution doctrines. MJIL 15:1–46")). Questions such as responsibility and decision-making authority in AI-related incidents, and the delegation of functions to AI entities will become increasingly common. Moreover, domestic rules apply only nationally (Cha, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR56 "Cha S \(2024\) Towards an international regulatory framework for AI safety: lessons from the IAEA’s nuclear safety regulations. HSSC 11:1–13")) while their the extraterritorial application is often seen as a form of legal imperialism (Wang, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR339 "Wang Y \(2024\) Do not go gentle into that good night: the European Union’s and China’s different approaches to the extraterritorial application of artificial intelligence laws and regulations. Comput Law Secur Rev 53:1–13")). Domestic regulations face criticism for failing to provide a unified definition of AI, adequately address key issues, and ensure compliance (Ratcliff, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR366 "Ratcliff CJ \(2023\) Who will write the rules for AI? How nations are racing to regulate artificial intelligence. The Conversation. 
                  https://theconversation.com/who-will-write-the-rules-for-ai-how-nations-are-racing-to-regulate-artificial-intelligence-216900
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). International organizations have begun recognizing the need for transnational AI regulations (Hars, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR144 "Hars A \(2022\) AI and international law—legal personality and avenues for regulation. Hung J Leg Stud 62:320–344. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1556/2052.2022.00352
                  
                ")), including private ones (Veale et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR323 "Veale M, Matus K, Gorwa R \(2023\) AI and global governance: modalities, rationales, tensions. Annu Rev Law Soc Sci 19:255–275. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-lawsocsci-020223-040749
                  
                ")), where limitations have been noticed (Nindler, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR230 "Nindler R \(2019\) The United Nation’s capability to manage existential risks with a focus on artificial intelligence. Int Community Law Rev 21\(1\):5–34. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1163/18719732-12341388
                  
                ")). Scholars emphasize the importance of such legislation, drawing parallels with fields like energy law (Huang et al., [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR157 "Huang K et al. \(2024\) AI regulations. In: Huang K, et al., eds Generative AI security: theories and practices. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 61–98")), highlighting specific concerns (Erman and Furendal, [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR98 "Erman E, Furendal M \(2022a\) Artificial intelligence and the political legitimacy of global governance. Polit Stud 1–21. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/00323217221126665
                  
                ")), and stressing the need for global collaboration (Feijoo et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR103 "Feijoo C et al. \(2020\) Harnessing artificial intelligence \(AI\) to increase wellbeing for all: the case for a new technology diplomacy. Telecom Policy 44:101988. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.telpol.2020.101988
                  
                ")). Relying solely on national approaches could lead to fragmented global regulations, as nations compete for AI development and potentially implement inadequate rules (Cihon, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR67 "Cihon P \(2019\) Standards for AI governance: International standards to enable global coordination in AI research & development. Future of Humanity Institute, University of Oxford")). Some cooperative efforts are already in place, such as transatlantic collaborations (Broadbent, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR38 "Broadbent M \(2021\) What’s ahead for a cooperative regulatory agenda on artificial intelligence? Center for Strategic & International Studies. 
                  https://www.csis.org/analysis/whats-ahead-cooperative-regulatory-agenda-artificial-intelligence#:~:text=The%20Commission%20hopes%20that%20the,in%20which%20AI%20is%20adopted
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). However, reaching a consensus on the values to include in global regulations remains challenging (HEC Paris Insights, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR146 "HEC Paris Insights \(2022\) Regulating artificial intelligence—is global consensus possible? Forbes. 
                  https://www.forbes.com/sites/hecparis/2022/09/09/regulating-artificial-intelligence--is-global-consensus-possible/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")), despite nations’ eagerness to lead these efforts (Meltzer, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR221 "Meltzer JP \(2023\) The US government should regulate AI if it wants to lead on international AI governance. Brookings. 
                  https://www.brookings.edu/articles/the-us-government-should-regulate-ai/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")) and call for global meetings (Baig, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR21 "Baig A \(2024\) An overview of emerging global AI regulations. 
                  https://securiti.ai/ai-regulations-around-the-world/
                  
                 Accessed 20 November 2024")). Overall, the extensive experience of international law can provide crucial support to a new field where these questions will arise (Burri, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR45 "Burri T \(2018\) International law and artificial intelligence. GYIL 60:91–108. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3790/gyil.60.1.91
                  
                ")).

## Theoretical framework

It centers on the way an AI regulatory authority would balance and organize the role of the different actors globally, including their powers and duties. This is a complicated task that requires great efforts to adopt laws that consider the interests and needs of all of them mainly states whose consent is crucial to establish and enforce AI rules domestically (Besson, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR28 "Besson S \(2016\) State consent and disagreement in international law-making. Dissolving the paradox. LJIL 29:289–316. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/S0922156516000030
                  
                ")).

Figure [1](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#Fig1) starts with the international organization responsible for regulating AI covering its various competences such as rulemaking, enforcement, and oversight. It also includes the representation of the different actors involved in the regulatory process mainly states, other organizations, private companies, and civil society. It is worth mentioning that some add other actors to this classification such as the market and the academic community. Those are considered as part of private companies and civil societies in this article (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")).

**Fig. 1**

[ Full size image](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x/figures/1)

Artificial Intelligence in the Context of Global Governance.

The question of who will regulate AI is closely tied to current global trends. As an emerging sector, it is already the subject of intense scholarly debate, with discussions about whether mechanisms similar to those currently tackling other fields should be used (Cogan, [2011](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR71 "Cogan JK \(2011\) The regulatory turn in international law. Harv Int’l L J 52:321–372"); Losifidis, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR203 "Losifidis P \(2016\) Globalisation and the re-emergence of the regulatory state. In: Flew T, Petros L, Steemers J \(eds.\) Global media and national policies. Palgrave Macmillan, London, pp 16–31")). This also involves examining the form that international institutional governance would take considering other challenges such as those related to sustainable development goals (Neuwirth, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR229 "Neuwirth RJ \(2024\) The global institutional governance of AI: a four-dimensional perspective. Int J Digit Law Gov 1–41. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1515/ijdlg-2024-0004
                  
                ")). The need for common standards and the creation of universal governance instruments is widely recognized (Cogan, [2011](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR71 "Cogan JK \(2011\) The regulatory turn in international law. Harv Int’l L J 52:321–372"); Losifidis, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR203 "Losifidis P \(2016\) Globalisation and the re-emergence of the regulatory state. In: Flew T, Petros L, Steemers J \(eds.\) Global media and national policies. Palgrave Macmillan, London, pp 16–31")). Its effect on governance is also acknowledged to prevent its disruptive effects from being overlooked or understated (McGregor, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR218 "McGregor L \(2018\) Accountability for governance choices in artificial intelligence: Afterword to Eyal Benvenisti’s foreword. 29:1079–1085. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ejil/chy086
                  
                ")). This has been examined highlighting a multitude of issues and challenges particularly the way international law will be impacted and the legal disruption that may occur (Liu et al., [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR201 "Liu HW et al. \(2020a\) Artificial intelligence and legal disruption: a new model for analysis. Law Innov Technol 12:205–258. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2020.1815402
                  
                ")).

This is happening in a context where the development of international law is a very complicated process that spans upon years and decades depending on the field and the sensitivity of the issue. This has been the case for instance for climate change (Peel, [2012](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR245 "Peel J \(2012\) Climate change law: the emergence of a new legal discipline. Melb Univ Law Rev 32:922–979")), transboundary water resources (McCaffrey, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR216 "McCaffrey SC \(2019\) The law of international watercourses. Oxford University Press, Oxford")) and other fields. The complication stems from the fact that states dominate the global arena given the absence of authority over them. Based on this, laws cannot be adopted without their consent (Klabbers, [2013](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR177 "Klabbers J \(2013\) International law. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")). Yet, nations have different interests they seek to either achieve or protect affecting the adoption as well as the implementation of laws. This reality led many to questioning the relevance of international law (Chinkin and Kaldor, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR65 "Chinkin C, Kaldor M \(2017\) International law and new wars. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")) and states responsibility under this framework as many nations violate regulatory frameworks without suffering any consequences (Hakimi, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR140 "Hakimi M \(2020\) Why should we care about international law? Mich Law Rev 118:1283–1306. 
                  https://doi.org/10.36644/mlr.118.6.why
                  
                ")). This is because the international community and global governance mechanisms do not have the necessary legitimacy to force a shift in a state’s behavior (Franzie, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR109 "Franzie B \(2018\) Does international law change state behavior? Submitted in partial completion of the requirements for departmental honors in political science. Bridgewater State University")). Hence, compliance is often seen the main challenge preventing the proper implementation of global rules (Howse and Teitel, [2010](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR155 "Howse R, Teitel R \(2010\) Beyond compliance: rethinking why international law really matters? Glob Policy 1:127–136. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1758-5899.2010.00035.x
                  
                ")). Nonetheless, noncompliance of specific nations does not mean that the system is completely inefficient as many others do obey the laws as their interests are not hindered by them (Pickering, [2014](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR248 "Pickering H \(2014\) Why do states mostly obey international law? E-Int Relat. 
                  https://www.e-ir.info/2014/02/04/why-do-states-mostly-obey-international-law/#:~:text=Essentially%2C%20states%20calculate%20their%20interests,states%20are%20likely%20to%20comply
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")), the existence of rewards for doing so such as cooperative gains and access to finance (Van Aaken and Simsek, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR319 "Van Aaken A, Simsek B \(2021\) Rewarding in international law. Am J Int Law 115:195–241. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/ajil.2021.2
                  
                ")) and simply being concerned about losing their reputation because of noncompliance (Brewster, [2009](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR37 "Brewster R \(2009\) Unpacking the state’s reputation. Harv Int’l L J 50:231–269")).

Scholars will disagree among each other over the shape, functions, and even whether an international structure should be created to address the global AI governance regime. This is a repeating scenario that has occurred every time the international community tried to develop regulatory frameworks. AI professionals and experts must learn from the mistakes made in other fields to avoid them.

## Literature review

The regulation of AI has been the subject of discussion (Goh and Vinuesa, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR127 "Goh HH, Vinuesa R \(2021\) Regulating artificial-intelligence applications to achieve the sustainable development goals. Discov Sustain 2:1–6. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43621-021-00064-5
                  
                ")), for several decades for now (Jackson, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR164 "Jackson BW \(2019\) Artificial intelligence and the fog of innovation: a deep-dive on governance and the liability of autonomous systems. St Clara High Technol Law J 35:35–63. 
                  https://digitalcommons.law.scu.edu/chtlj/vol35/iss4/1
                  
                ")), where a race towards doing so among the different nations is taking place (Broadbent, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR38 "Broadbent M \(2021\) What’s ahead for a cooperative regulatory agenda on artificial intelligence? Center for Strategic & International Studies. 
                  https://www.csis.org/analysis/whats-ahead-cooperative-regulatory-agenda-artificial-intelligence#:~:text=The%20Commission%20hopes%20that%20the,in%20which%20AI%20is%20adopted
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Competition occurs also between countries and supra-national institutions such as the Council of Europe and the United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization (Oprea et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR235 "Oprea O, Hoinaru R, Pacuraru-Lonescu CP, Neamtu D \(2022\) Accounting for the future: practice, Artificial Intelligence and regulation. Sciendo. 817–826. 
                  https://doi.org/10.2478/picbe-2022-0076
                  
                ")). Regardless, the question of the most appropriate way to regulate AI (Bensoussan, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR26 "Bensousan A \(2020\) How to legally regulate artificial intelligence? Ann Mines 12:1–3")), and whether laws are needed remain unanswered (Han et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR141 "Han TA, Pereira LM, Santos FC, Lenaerts T \(2020\) To regulate or not: a social dynamics analysis of an 27tandardi AI race. JAIR 69:881–921. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1613/jair.1.12225
                  
                ")). What is certain is that lawmakers must possess great understanding of AI issues to adopt appropriate rules (Vinuesa et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR332 "Vinuesa R et al. \(2020\) The role of artificial intelligence in achieving the Sustainable Development Goals. Nat Commun 11:1–10. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-019-14108-y
                  
                ")), as the traditional ones are being challenged (Du, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR91 "Du M \(2023\) Policy regulation of artificial intelligence: a review of the literature. In: Tallón-Ballesteros AJ, Santana-Morales P \(eds\) Digitalization and management innovation. IOS Press, Amsterdam, p 407–415")). The interplay between the law and AI as a subject is not novel if one considers the literature related to regulations and technology over the decades (Dellapenna, [2000](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR84 "Dellapenna JW \(2000\) Law in a shrinking world: The interaction of science and technology with international law. Ky L J 88:809–883")), particularly this topic (Governatori et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR132 "Governatori G et al. \(2022\) Thirty years of artificial intelligence and law: the first decade. Artif Intell Law 30:481–519. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10506-022-09329-4
                  
                "); Sartor et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR264 "Sartor et al. \(2022\) Thirty years of artificial intelligence and law: the second decade. Artif Intell Law 30:521–557. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10506-022-09326-7
                  
                "); Villata et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR331 "Villata S et al. \(2022\) Thirty years of artificial intelligence and law: the third decade. Artif Intell Law 30:561–591. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10506-022-09327-6
                  
                ")) where governance represents one fundamental aspect tackled (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")). The role of international law in the progress of the AI field (Castel and Castel, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR52 "Castel JG, Castel ME \(2016\) The road to artificial super-intelligence: has international law a role to play? CJLT 14:1–15")) is widely debated (Nash, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR228 "Nash L \(2019\) Advancing intelligence and global society: International law’s role in governing the advance of artificial intelligence. Ky Law J. Online. 108. 
                  https://perma.cc/I4-24CJ
                  
                ")), in relation to its fundamental principles, as well as questions of ownership and liability (Tzimas, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR306 "Tzimas T \(2021\) Legal and ethical challenges of artificial intelligence from an international law perspective. Springer, Dordrecht")).

AI rules are conceived as means for the protection of public interests and ethics as well as avoiding and tackling existing inequalities resulting from such technologies (Gantzias, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR113 "Gantzias G \(2020\) Dynamics of public interest in artificial intelligence: ‘Business intelligence culture’ and global regulation in the digital era. In: Park SH, Gonzalez-Perez MA, Floriani DE \(eds\) The Palgrave handbook of corporate sustainability in the digital era. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 259–281")). This is very much needed considering the ambiguity surrounding the rules associated with this field across continents (Walker-Osborn and Chan, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR335 "Walker-Osborn C, Chan C \(2017\) Artificial intelligence and the law. ITNOW 59:36–37. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/itnow/bwx017
                  
                ")), despite the huge complexities related to this discussion (Dremliuga et al., [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR89 "Dremliuga RI et al. \(2019\) Artificial intelligence as a subject of law: pros and cons. Dilemas Contemporáneos: Educación. Política Valores 7:1–16")). The obstacles are the result of a lack of consensus over the definition of the term (Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR276 "Smuha NA \(2021\) From a ‘race to AI’ to a ‘race to AI regulation’: regulatory competition for artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 13:57–84. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2021.1898300
                  
                ")) and the different regulatory theories that can apply mainly those related to the public interest; conspiracy; organizational and capitalist state theories (Gantzias, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR113 "Gantzias G \(2020\) Dynamics of public interest in artificial intelligence: ‘Business intelligence culture’ and global regulation in the digital era. In: Park SH, Gonzalez-Perez MA, Floriani DE \(eds\) The Palgrave handbook of corporate sustainability in the digital era. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 259–281")). This in addition to the broad nature of this sector impacting other fields given the existence of various AI techniques and applications and the regulatory race where various legal regimes are impacted (Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR276 "Smuha NA \(2021\) From a ‘race to AI’ to a ‘race to AI regulation’: regulatory competition for artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 13:57–84. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2021.1898300
                  
                ")). These include international trade law; international criminal law (Borlini, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR34 "Borlini L \(2023\) Economic interventionism and international trade law in the covid era. GLJ 24:1–16. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/glj.2023.13
                  
                ")); international economic law (Peng et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR246 "Peng SY, Lin CF, Streinz T \(2021\) Artificial intelligence and international economic law: disruption, regulation and reconfiguration. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")); international human rights law (Roumate, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR259 "Roumate F \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, ethics and international human rights law. IRIE 29:1–10"); Langford, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR189 "Langford M \(2020\) Taming the digital leviathan: automated decision-making and international human rights. Am J Int Law 114:141–146. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/aju.2020.31
                  
                ")) where rights are discussed even for robots for instance (Gellers, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR118 "Gellers J \(2020\) Rights for robots: artificial intelligence, animal and environmental law. Routledge, Abingdon")); intellectual property rights (Hashiguchi, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR145 "Hashiguchi M \(2017\) The global artificial intelligence revolution challenges patent eligibility laws. JBTL 13:1–35. 
                  https://digitalcommons.law.umaryland.edu/jbtl/vol13/iss1/2
                  
                ")); international humanitarian law (Hua, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR156 "Hua SS \(2019\) Machine learning weapons and international humanitarian law: rethinking meaningful human control. Georget J Int Law 51:117–146")); international nuclear security law (Anastassov, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR17 "Anastassov A \(2021\) Artificial intelligence and its possible use in international nuclear security law. Papers of BAS. Hum Soc Sci 8:92–103")); relation between international law and regional rules such as European Union (EU) law considering this issue (Committee on Legal Affairs [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR72 "Committee on Legal Affairs \(2021\) Report on artificial intelligence: questions of interpretation and application of international law in so far as the EU is affected in the areas of civil and military uses and of state authority outside the scope of criminal justice. European Parliament. 
                  https://www.europarl.europa.eu/doceo/document/A-9-2021-0001_EN.html
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); The Editors of the EAPIL, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR295 "The Editors of the EAPIL \(2021\) The EU’s upcoming regulatory framework on artificial intelligence and its Impact on PIL. EAPIL. 
                  https://eapil.org/2021/07/12/the-eus-upcoming-regulatory-framework-on-artificial-intelligence-and-its-impact-on-pil/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")) even from a global private regulatory framework perspective (Poesen, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR249 "Poesen M \(2022\) Regulating artificial intelligence \(AI\) in the European Union \(EU\): Exploring the role of privateinternational law. In Boruchi C et al \(eds\) Recht in Beweging: 29ste VRG – Alumnidag 2022. Gompel & Svacina, Antwerp, pp 297–314")). All these norms and rules represent international law’s initial response to AI (Zekos, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR361 "Zekos GI \(2021\) Economics and law of artificial intelligence: finance, economic impacts, risk management and governance. Springer, Dordrecht")), as the rules often follow technological developments (Payne, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR244 "Payne K \(2018\) Artificial intelligence: a revolution in strategic affairs? Survival 60:7–32. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/00396338.2018.1518374
                  
                ")), considering the multilateral context and the different institutions (Garcia, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR117 "Garcia EV \(2021\) Multilateralism and artificial intelligence: what role for the United Nations? 
                  https://www.researchgate.net/publication/350193708_Multilateralism_and_Artificial_Intelligence_What_Role_for_the_United_Nations
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

The Impact of technology on international law is relevant in terms of shaping and changing it (Deeks, [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR82 "Deeks A \(2020a\) High-tech international law. Geo Wash L Rev 88:574–653")), especially with regards to contributing to its establishment, enforcement (Deeks [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR82 "Deeks A \(2020a\) High-tech international law. Geo Wash L Rev 88:574–653"), [2020b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR83 "Deeks A \(2020b\) Introduction to the symposium: How will artificial intelligence affect international law? AJIL 114:138–140. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/aju.2020.29
                  
                ")) and the decision-making process (Arvidsson and Noll, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR20 "Arvidsson M, Noll G \(2023\) Artificial intelligence, decision making and international law. Nord J Int Law 92:1–8")). This is in contrast to others who claim that AI may result in the displacement even destruction of international law (Maas, [2019a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR205 "Maas M \(2019a\) International law does not compute: artificial intelligence and the development, displacement or destruction of the global legal order. Melb J Int Law 29:1–29")) or the revaluation of the existing rules (Zekos, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR361 "Zekos GI \(2021\) Economics and law of artificial intelligence: finance, economic impacts, risk management and governance. Springer, Dordrecht")), as the law reaches its limits in its regulatory attempts (Alschner, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR10 "Alschner W \(2021\) The computational analysis of international law. In: Deplano R, Tsagourias N \(eds\) Research methods in international law: a handbook. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham, pp 203–227")). In short, some claim that reliance on public international law would not yield the expected results in terms of controlling and tackling AI developments (Liu, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR200 "Liu HY \(2018\) The power structure of artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 10:197–229. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2018.1527480
                  
                ")), as structural changes to this framework are being noticed (Burchardt, [2023a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR43 "Burchardt D \(2023a\) Editorial to the special issue “the impact of digitalization on international law”. GLJ 24:435–437. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/glj.2023.32
                  
                ")). These include the empowerment and emergence of new public and private actors beyond the nation; changing power dynamics among states; increasing fragmentation and legal gaps as well as challenging and reinforcing values simultaneously (Burchardt, [2023b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR44 "Burchardt D \(2023b\) Does digitalization change international law structurally? GLJ 24:438–460. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/glj.2023.31
                  
                ")). Moreover, questions with regards to AI’s potential personality (Hárs, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR144 "Hars A \(2022\) AI and international law—legal personality and avenues for regulation. Hung J Leg Stud 62:320–344. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1556/2052.2022.00352
                  
                ")), and its limits (Chesterman, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR61 "Chesterman S \(2020\) Artificial intelligence and the limits of legal personality. ICLQ 69:819–844. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/S0020589320000366
                  
                ")) as well as its legal classification as global commons are taking place (Tzimas, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR305 "Tzimas T \(2018\) Artificial intelligence as global commons and the “international law supremacy” principle. In: Striełkowski W, et al., \(eds\) Advances in social science, education and humanities research. Atlantis. Press, Dordrecht")).

Given this reality, scholars are wondering whether more or less rules are needed (Woodcock, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR350 "Woodcock T \(2021\) Artificial intelligence: is the answer more law? Asser Institute. 
                  https://www.asser.nl/about-the-asser-institute/news/blog-artificial-intelligence-is-the-answer-more-law/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")), if a balance can be achieved in the short and long term (McCarty, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR217 "McCarty LT \(2018\) Finding the right balance in artificial intelligence and law. In: Barfield W, Pagallo U \(eds\) Research handbook on the law of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham, p 55–87")), and whether new concepts are required to tackle the disruption taking place in the legal field due to AI (Liu et al., [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR201 "Liu HW et al. \(2020a\) Artificial intelligence and legal disruption: a new model for analysis. Law Innov Technol 12:205–258. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2020.1815402
                  
                ")), where current jurisprudence is perceived as inadequate (Paliwala, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR242 "Paliwala A \(2016\) Rediscovering artificial intelligence and law: An inadequate jurisprudence? Law Innov Technol 30:107–114. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/13600869.2016.1229651
                  
                ")). In this context, AI principles have been identified (Weber, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR340 "Weber RH \(2021\) Artificial intelligence ante portas: reactions of law. J 4:486–499. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3390/j4030037
                  
                ")). Self-regulation initiative by tech companies have emerged given the absence of a globally established framework and the private sector push towards minimal rules (Butcher and Beridze, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR47 "Butcher JG, Beridze I \(2019\) What is the state of artificial intelligence governance globally? RUSI 164:88–96. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694260
                  
                ")). A global agreement on AI is not currently in place (Mishalchenko et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR224 "Mishalchenko YV, Dovbush TN, Mishalchenko MY \(2022\) International legal and economic aspects of regulation of digital technologies and artificial intelligence. In: Rumyantseva A, et al., \(eds\) Challenges and solutions in the digital economy and finance. Springer, Dordrecht, p 29–36")), as the fragmented global scene into multiple institutions and existing norms further complicates this situation (Schmitt, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR268 "Schmitt L \(2022\) Mapping global AI governance: a nascent regime in a fragmented landscape. AI Ethics 2:303–314. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43681-021-00083-y
                  
                ")). Overall, proposals range from existing norms and instruments to the creation of new rules and institutions (Koniakou, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR179 "Koniakou V \(2023\) From the “rush to ethics” to the “race for governance” in artificial intelligence. Inf Syst Front 25:71–102. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10796-022-10300-6
                  
                ")). Some scholars have developed specific conceptual frameworks to that end stating the rational for doing so (de Almeida et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR81 "De Almeida PGR, Dos Santos CD, Farias JS \(2021\) Artificial intelligence regulation: a framework for governance. Ethics Inf Technol 23:505–525. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10676-021-09593-z
                  
                ")). Others called for the establishment of a global standard for AI regulation (Wu and Liu, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR355 "Wu W, Liu S \(2023\) The need for a global standard of artificial intelligence regulation. In Proceedings of ACM SIGCAS computers and society. 52")), while some consider regional rules such as the EU Act as a potential global regulation (Musch et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR227 "Musch S, Borrelli M, Kerrigan C \(2023\) The EU AI act as global artificial intelligence regulation")). This is part of a larger debate where the focus is on the best way to regulate this field (de Almeida et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR80 "De Almeida PGR, Dos Santos CD, Farias JS \(2020\) Artificial intelligence regulation: a meta-framework for formulation and governance. In Proceedings of the 53rd Hawaii international conference on system sciences")). Approaches such as pragmatism to this matter are being considered (Ellul et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR94 "Ellul J et al. \(2021\) A pragmatic approach to regulating artificial intelligence: A technology regulator’s perspective")), in addition to whether regulations should target specific sectors or individual areas (Finocchiaro, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR105 "Finocchiaro G \(2023\) The regulation of artificial intelligence. AI & Society. 1–8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-023-01650-z
                  
                ")). Additionally, conversations concerning the adoption of strict or soft rules (Papyshev and Yarime, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR243 "Papyshev G, Yarime M \(2022\) The limitation of ethics-based approaches to regulating artificial intelligence: regulatory gifting in the context of Russia. AI Soc. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-022-01611-y
                  
                ")), the use of regulatory sandboxes and risk based approaches are underway (Díaz-Rodríguez, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR86 "Diaz-Rodriguez N et al. \(2023\) Connecting the dots in trustworthy artificial intelligence: from AI principles, ethics, and key requirements to responsible AI systems and regulation. Inf Fusion 99:1–24. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.inffus.2023.101896
                  
                ")).

Overall, scholars are debating the efficiency of regulations to tackle AI (O’Halloran and Nowaczyk, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR234 "O’Halloran S, Nowaczyk N \(2019\) An Artificial intelligence approach to regulating systemic risk. Front Artif Intell 2:1–14. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3389/frai.2019.00007
                  
                ")), and their impact on its subjects: states, natural persons, and corporations (Lee, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR191 "Lee J \(2022\) Artificial intelligence and international law. Springer, Dordrecht")), claiming that smart regulations are the way forward (Hillman, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR149 "Hillman J \(2023\) Smart regulation: Lessons from the artificial intelligence act. EILR 37:775–826. 
                  https://scholarlycommons.law.emory.edu/eilr/vol37/iss4/6
                  
                ")). Others claim that the approach of wait and see is the most effective one (Villarino and Vijeyarasa, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR326 "Villarino JMB, Vijeyarasa R \(2022\) International human rights, artificial intelligence, and the challenge for the pondering state: time to regulate? Nord J Hum Rights 40:194–215. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/18918131.2022.2069919
                  
                ")). AI affects various topics such as human rights, sovereignty, state responsibility, dispute settlement, war, North-South Divide (Lee, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR191 "Lee J \(2022\) Artificial intelligence and international law. Springer, Dordrecht")), the rule of law (Greenstein, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR133 "Greenstein S \(2022\) Preserving the rule of law in the era of artificial intelligence \(AI\). Artif Intell Law 30:291–323. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10506-021-09294-4
                  
                ")); democracy (Leslie et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR194 "Leslie D et al. \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, human rights, democracy, and the rule of law—a primer. Council of Europe. 
                  https://edoc.coe.int/en/artificial-intelligence/10206-artificial-intelligence-human-rights-democracy-and-the-rule-of-law-a-primer.html
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")); corporate responsibility (Lane, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR188 "Lane L \(2023\) Artificial intelligence and human rights: corporate responsibility under international human rights law. In: Završnik A, Simončič K \(eds\) Artificial intelligence, social harms and human rights. Springer, Dordrecht, p 183–205")); autonomous weapons (Piccone, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR247 "Piccone T \(2018\) How can international law regulate autonomous weapons? Brookings. 
                  https://www.brookings.edu/articles/how-can-international-law-regulate-autonomous-weapons/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")); the judicial system overall (Said et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR260 "Said G et al. \(2023\) Adapting legal systems to the development of artificial intelligence: solving the global problem of AI in judicial processes. Int J Cyber Law 1:1–17. 
                  https://doi.org/10.59022/ijcl.49
                  
                ")); weapons of mass destruction (Chesterman, [2021a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR62 "Chesterman S \(2021a\) Weapons of mass disruption: artificial intelligence and international law. CILJ 10:181–203. 
                  https://doi.org/10.4337/cilj.2021.02.02
                  
                ")); discrimination (Packin and Aretz, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR239 "Packin NG, Aretz YL \(2018\) Learning algorithms and discrimination. In: Barfield W, Pagallo U \(eds\) Research handbook of law and AI. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")); autonomous shipping (Soyer and Tettenborn, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR279 "Soyer B, Tettenborn A \(2021\) Artificial intelligence and autonomous shipping: developing the international legal framework. Hart Publishing, Oxford")); robots (Humble, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR158 "Humble K \(2023\) Artificial intelligence, international law and the race for killer robots in modern warfare. In: Završnik A, Simončič Katja \(eds\) Artificial intelligence, social harms and human rights. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 57–76")); space (Martin and Freeland, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR211 "Martin AS, Freeland S \(2021\) The advent of artificial intelligence in space activities: new legal challenges. Space Policy 55:101408. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.spacepol.2020.101408
                  
                ")); health (Murdoch, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR226 "Murdoch B \(2021\) Privacy and artificial intelligence: challenges for protecting health information in a new era. BMC Med Ethics 22:1–5. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1186/s12910-021-00687-3
                  
                ")); law enforcement (Alzou’bi et al., [2014](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR15 "Alzou’bi S, Alshibly H, Al-Ma’aitah \(2014\) Artificial intelligence in law enforcement, a review. IJAIT 4:1–9. 
                  https://doi.org/10.5121/ijait.2014.4401
                  
                ")); liability (Kingston, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR174 "Kingston J \(2018\) Artificial intelligence and legal liability. Cornell University")); justice (Bex et al., [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR29 "Bex F, Prakken H, Van Engers T, Bart V \(2017\) Introduction to the special issue on Artificial Intelligence for Justice \(AI4J\). Artif Intell Law 25:1–3. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10506-017-9198-5
                  
                ")) and environmental concerns (Qerimi and Sergi, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR251 "Qerimi Q, Sergi BS \(2022\) The case for global regulation of carbon capture and storage and artificial intelligence for climate change. Int J Greenh Gas Control 120:103757. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijggc.2022.103757
                  
                ")). Challenges overall (Abashidze et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR1 "Abashidze AKH, Inshakova AO, Dementev AA \(2021\) New challenges of international law in the digital age. In: Popkova EG, Sergi BS \(eds\) Modern global economic system: evolutional development vs. revolutionary leap. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 1125–1132")) especially to the application of laws such as its constant development; lack of transparency, the ability to avoid legal limitations (Hoffman-Riem [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR367 "Hoffman-Riem W \(2020\) Artificial intelligence as a challenge for law and regulation. In: Wischmeyer T, Rademacher T \(eds\) Regulating artificial intelligence. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 1–29")); lack of accountability (McGregor, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR218 "McGregor L \(2018\) Accountability for governance choices in artificial intelligence: Afterword to Eyal Benvenisti’s foreword. 29:1079–1085. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ejil/chy086
                  
                ")) are noticed.

It is worth mentioning that for the time being, international law lags behind when it comes to the adoption of appropriate rules for governing AI (Chache et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR57 "Chache EG, Dremlyuga RI, Tret’yakova NA, Neznamov AV \(2023\) System approach to the study of state policies and formation of ethics of application of artificial intelligence technologies: global atlas of regulation. Dokl Math 106:S65–S69. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1134/S1064562422060059
                  
                ")), given the complexity surrounding the adoption of a suitable regulatory design especially concerning the question of the exact time to make a regulatory intervention (Walz and Firth-Butterfield, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR337 "Walz A, Firth-Butterfield K \(2019\) Implementing ethics into artificial intelligence: a contribution, from a legal perspective, to the development of an AI governance regime. Duke Law Technol Rev 18:176–231. 
                  https://scholarship.law.duke.edu/dltr/vol18/iss1/17
                  
                ")). The result was suggestions of adopting hybrid governance perspective via public-private partnerships (Radu, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR252 "Radu R \(2021\) Steering the governance of artificial intelligence: national strategies in perspective. Policy Soc 40:178–193. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/14494035.2021.1929728
                  
                ")). This gains further importance when one considers the calls for ensuring that humans govern through laws and not AI (Gantzias, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR114 "Gantzias G \(2021\) Governing by humans, not by robots: regulating humans and artificial intelligence in the 21st century. In: Christiansen B, Škrinjarić T \(eds\) Handbook of research on applied AI for international business and marketing applications. IGI Global, Pennsylvania, pp 116–134")), and the need for flexibility (Johnson, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR168 "Johnson WG \(2022\) Flexible regulation for dynamic products? The case of applying principles-based regulation to medical products using artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 14:205–236. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2022.2113665
                  
                ")). The topic is becoming increasingly political (Tinnirello, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR297 "Tinnirello M \(2022\) The global politics of artificial intelligence. Routledge, Abingdon")), particularly given the diverging public views associated with its regulation (Bartneck et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR22 "Bartneck C, Yogeeswaran K, Sibley CG \(2023\) Personality and demographic correlates of support for regulating artificial intelligence. AI and Ethics. 1–8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43681-023-00279-4
                  
                ")), society’s responsibility (Hilton, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR150 "Hilton AD \(2019\) Artificial intelligence: the societal responsibility to inform, educate, and regulate. AI Matters 5:70–76. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1145/3362077.3362088
                  
                ")), the limits of law and emerging dilemmas (Chesterman, [2021b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR63 "Chesterman S \(2021b\) We, the robots? Regulating artificial intelligence and the limits of the law. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")).

## Overview of most pertinent legal initiatives and regulations

The European Parliament and Council passed a regulation in 2023, establishing harmonized rules for AI. This legislation encompasses general provisions and identifies prohibited AI practices. It classifies AI systems by risk, specifying stringent requirements for those deemed high-risk. It outlines responsibilities for providers, users, and other stakeholders involved with high-risk AI systems. The act details the roles of notifying authorities and notified bodies, and sets standards for assessments, certifications, and registrations. It mandates transparency for certain AI systems and supports innovative measures. Governance structures are put in place, including the European AI Board, national competent authorities, and an EU database for standalone high-risk AI systems. The regulation provides for post-market monitoring, facilitates the sharing of information on incidents and malfunctions, and ensures market surveillance. It also introduces codes of conduct, confidentiality requirements, penalties, and outlines procedures for delegation of power and committee operations (EU AI Act, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR364 "EU AI Act \(2024\) European Parliament legislative resolution of 13 March 2024 on the proposal for a regulation of the European Parliament and of the Council on laying down harmonised rules on Artificial Intelligence \(Artificial Intelligence Act\) and amending certain Union Legislative Acts \(COM\(2021\)0206 – C9-0146/2021 – 2021/0106\(COD\)\)")).

The Council of Europe established a Committee on Artificial Intelligence (CAI) to lead international negotiations aimed at creating a comprehensive legal framework. This involves facilitating discussions among nations in line with the UN sustainable development agenda (CAI, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR368 "Committee on Artificial Intelligence \(CAI\) \(2021\) Ad hoc Committee. 
                  https://rm.coe.int/terms-of-reference-of-the-committee-on-artificial-intelligence-for-202/1680a74d2f
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). In 2023, the Committee published a Revised Zero Draft [Framework] Convention on Artificial Intelligence, Human Rights, Democracy, and the Rule of Law (Council of Europe, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR74 "Council of Europe \(2024\) Framework Convention on Artificial Intelligence, Human Rights, Democracy and the Rule of Law")). This document includes became a binding treaty covering general provisions and obligations; principles related to activities within the lifecycle of artificial intelligence systems; remedies; assessment and mitigation of risks and adverse impacts; convention implementation; follow-up mechanism and cooperation and final clauses (Council of Europe, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR74 "Council of Europe \(2024\) Framework Convention on Artificial Intelligence, Human Rights, Democracy and the Rule of Law")).

The UN Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization (UNESCO) adopted recommendations on the ethics of AI, covering various systems primarily related to education, science, culture, communication, and information. These aim to establish a global framework comprising values, principles, and actions to guide stakeholders in achieving multiple goals. The core values emphasized are respect, protection, and promotion of human rights, fundamental freedoms, and human dignity; environmental and ecosystem health; diversity and inclusiveness; and fostering peaceful, just, and interconnected societies. The principles include proportionality and non-harm; safety and security; fairness and non-discrimination; sustainability; privacy and data protection; human oversight and determination; transparency and explainability; responsibility and accountability; awareness and literacy; and multi-stakeholder, adaptive governance and collaboration. The policy action areas include ethical impact assessments, ethical governance and stewardship, data policies, development and international cooperation, environmental and ecosystem considerations, gender issues, cultural impacts, education and research, communication and information, economic and labor considerations, and health and social well-being (UNESCO, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR369 "United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization \(2021\) Recommendation on the ethics of artificial intelligence. 
                  https://www.unesco.org/en/articles/recommendation-ethics-artificial-intelligence?
                  
                ")).

The People’s Republic of China has enacted several laws concerning AI, particularly since 2021 (Kachra, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR170 "Kachra AJ \(2024\) Making sense of China’s AI regulations. 
                  https://www.holisticai.com/blog/china-ai-regulation#:~:text=Drafted%20by%20the%20Cyberspace%20Administration,tags%20about%20their%20personal%20characteristics
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). These include the 2023 Deep Synthesis Provisions, which are expected to regulate AI-generated content at every stage until its publication (Cyberspace Administration of China, [2023a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR75 "Cyberspace Administration of China \(2023a\) 国家互联网信息办公室等三部门发布《互联网信息服务深度合成管理规定》 \(Deep Synthesis Provisions\). 
                  https://www.cac.gov.cn/2022-12/11/c_1672221949318230.htm
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). The Internet Information Service Algorithmic Recommendation Management Provisions, enforced in 2022, aim to protect user rights in mobile applications by prohibiting fake accounts and the manipulation of traffic numbers (Creemers et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR76 "Creemer R, Webster G, Toner H \(2022\) Translation: Internet Information Service Algorithmic Recommendation Management Provisions—Effective March 1, 2022. 
                  https://digichina.stanford.edu/work/translation-internet-information-service-algorithmic-recommendation-management-provisions-effective-march-1-2022/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Additionally, China adopted the Interim Measures for Generative Artificial Intelligence Service Management, through which legislators seek to balance innovation with legal governance based on specific principles (Cyberspace Administration of China, [2023b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR77 "Cyberspace Administration of China \(2023b\) 生成式人工智能服务管理暂行办法 \(Interim Measures for Generative Artificial Intelligence Service Management\). 
                  https://www.cac.gov.cn/2023-07/13/c_1690898327029107.htm
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

The United States adopted several federal regulations concerning AI. These include the White House Executive Order on AI, which covers numerous sectors, and the White House AI Bill of Rights, which lays down specific principles (White & Case, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR370 "White & Case LLP \(2024\) AI watch: Global regulatory tracker - United States. 
                  https://www.whitecase.com/insight-our-thinking/ai-watch-global-regulatory-tracker-united-states#:~:text=As%20noted%20above
                  
                %2C%20there%20is,Responsible%20innovation%20and%20development. Accessed 20 November 2024")). Additionally, various state legislations have been adopted concerning safety, data privacy, transparency, accountability, and other topics. Overall, the number of federal laws and state rules on this topic has tremendously increased over the years, with some states, such as California, being more ambitious than others (Lerude, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR193 "Lerude B \(2023\) States take the lead on regulating artificial intelligence. 
                  https://www.brennancenter.org/our-work/research-reports/states-take-lead-regulating-artificial-intelligence
                  
                . Brennan Center for Justice. Accessed 20 November 2024")).

## Artificial intelligence, international law, and global actors

This section will examine the role of the various relevant stakeholders in the creation of international law applicable to AI and a regulatory authority. The purpose is to highlight their role in the process that has already been initiated.

### States

States represent the most relevant rule-makers in the global arena (Chesterman [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR63 "Chesterman S \(2021b\) We, the robots? Regulating artificial intelligence and the limits of the law. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")). They are the main actor under international law with the authority to agree and implement global rules domestically. Treaties established by nations are used to organize relations among them concerning specific issues (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")), considering each one’s sovereignty, rights, and duties (Pagallo et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR241 "Pagallo U, Bassi E, Durante M \(2023\) The normative challenges of AI in outer space: law, ethics, and the realignment of terrestrial standards. Philos Technol 36:23–23. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s13347-023-00626-7
                  
                ")). Nations are held accountable by other ones for their actions or lack of where current rapid changes especially from a technological perspective are complicating the ability to regulate the evolving landscape (Weiss [2011](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR371 "Weiss EB \(2011\) International law in the kaleidoscopic world. Am J Int Law 1:21–32")). This includes AI (Langford, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR189 "Langford M \(2020\) Taming the digital leviathan: automated decision-making and international human rights. Am J Int Law 114:141–146. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/aju.2020.31
                  
                ")), being used by nations to impact global developments (Hwang and Rosen, [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR160 "Hwang T, Rosen L \(2017\) Harder, better, faster, stronger: international law and the future of online PsyOps. Com Prop Working Paper No.1. University of Oxford")), towards a more desirable future considering various factors and values (Gabriel, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR111 "Gabriel I \(2020\) Artificial intelligence, values, and alignment. Minds Mach 30:411–437. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11023-020-09539-2
                  
                ")) and the potential changes affecting the shaping of the legal regime (Maas, [2019b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR206 "Maas MM \(2019b\) Innovation-proof global governance for military artificial intelligence? How I learned to stop worrying, and love the bot. J Int Humanit Leg Stud 10:129–157. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1163/18781527-01001006
                  
                ")).
