In fact, governments are debating whether such framework is needed, the approach to use and the actors to be involved (Cath, [2018a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR53 "Cath C \(2018a\) Governing artificial intelligence: ethical, legal and technical opportunities and challenges. Philos Trans R Soc A 376:1–8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1098/rsta.2018.0080
                  
                ")), as political legitimacy plays an important role (Erman and Furendal, [2022a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR98 "Erman E, Furendal M \(2022a\) Artificial intelligence and the political legitimacy of global governance. Polit Stud 1–21. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/00323217221126665
                  
                ")) in addition to balancing innovation with risks (Miailhe and Lannquist, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR223 "Miailhe N, Lannquist Y \(2022\) Global governance of artificial intelligence. In: Naqvi A, Munoz JM \(eds\) Handbook of artificial intelligence and robotic process automation policy and government applications. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, p 23–30")).

Given the absence of an actual dialog among nations albeit collaboration is seen as the way forward, its regulation remains difficult despite the need for it with the increasing sophistication and interconnectedness of AI systems (Butcher and Beridze, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR47 "Butcher JG, Beridze I \(2019\) What is the state of artificial intelligence governance globally? RUSI 164:88–96. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694260
                  
                "); Sari and Celik, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR263 "Sari O, Celik S \(2021\) Legal evaluation of the attacks caused by artificial intelligence-based lethal weapon systems within the context of Rome statute. Comput Law Secur Rev 42:105564. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.clsr.2021.105564
                  
                ")), as well as its growing impact on the global economy (Saidakhrarovich and Sokhibjonovich, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR261 "Saidakhrarovich GS, Sokhibjonovich BS \(2022\) Strategies and future prospects of development of artificial intelligence: world experience. World Bull Manag Law 9:66–74. 
                  https://www.scholarexpress.net/index.php/wbml/article/view/841
                  
                ")). States are invited to provide suggestions on how to move forward (Roumate, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR259 "Roumate F \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, ethics and international human rights law. IRIE 29:1–10")), while simultaneously using the new technological tools to implement international law (Deeks, [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR82 "Deeks A \(2020a\) High-tech international law. Geo Wash L Rev 88:574–653")), and address whether regulations necessitate amendments (Zekos, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR361 "Zekos GI \(2021\) Economics and law of artificial intelligence: finance, economic impacts, risk management and governance. Springer, Dordrecht")). Existing regulatory frameworks require an accelerated adaptation and expansion to address the constantly evolving digitalization activities posing new questions for humanity and affecting old ones (Abashidze et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR1 "Abashidze AKH, Inshakova AO, Dementev AA \(2021\) New challenges of international law in the digital age. In: Popkova EG, Sergi BS \(eds\) Modern global economic system: evolutional development vs. revolutionary leap. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 1125–1132")). This is of outmost importance as AI will affect sovereignty, social, cultural, economic and political norms where the effect differs from developed to developing nations (Usman, Nawaz and Naseer [n.d.](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR381 "Usman H, Nawaz B, Naseer S. \(n.d.\) The future of state sovereignty in the age of artificial intelligence. J Law Soc Stud. 5:142–152. 
                  https://doi.org/10.52279/jlss.05.02.142152
                  
                ")). This resulted in calls for a precautionary approach concerning this topic to balance opportunities and uncertainties (Leslie et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR194 "Leslie D et al. \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, human rights, democracy, and the rule of law—a primer. Council of Europe. 
                  https://edoc.coe.int/en/artificial-intelligence/10206-artificial-intelligence-human-rights-democracy-and-the-rule-of-law-a-primer.html
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")), when attempting to establish rules by governments (Tóth et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR298 "Toth Z, Caruana R, Gruber T, Loebbecke C \(2022\) The dawn of the AI robots: towards a new framework of AI robot accountability. J Bus Ethics 178:895–916. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10551-022-05050-z
                  
                ")), that need to comply with said regulations (Yara et al., [2021a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR356 "Yara O, Brazheyev A, Golovko L, Bashkatova V \(2021a\) Legal regulation of the use of Artificial Intelligence: problems and development prospects. Eur J Sustain Dev 10:281–289. 
                  https://doi.org/10.14207/ejsd.2021.v10n1p281
                  
                ")).

The regime to be installed, the values and principles, the decision-making process haven’t yet been discussed collectively among states (Erman and Furendal, [2022a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR98 "Erman E, Furendal M \(2022a\) Artificial intelligence and the political legitimacy of global governance. Polit Stud 1–21. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/00323217221126665
                  
                "), [2022b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR99 "Erman E, Furendal M \(2022b\) The global governance of artificial intelligence: some normative concerns. MOPP 9:267–291. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1515/mopp-2020-0046
                  
                ")). These are expected to affect many domestic legal aspects of AI development (Maas, [2019b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR206 "Maas MM \(2019b\) Innovation-proof global governance for military artificial intelligence? How I learned to stop worrying, and love the bot. J Int Humanit Leg Stud 10:129–157. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1163/18781527-01001006
                  
                ")). Factors to be considered include institutional barriers; fragmentation of international law, geopolitical realities (Sepasspour, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR275 "Sepasspour R \(2023\) A reality check and a way forward for the global governance of artificial intelligence. Bull Sci 79:304–315. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/00963402.2023.2245249
                  
                ")), human rights, economic considerations (Abashidze et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR1 "Abashidze AKH, Inshakova AO, Dementev AA \(2021\) New challenges of international law in the digital age. In: Popkova EG, Sergi BS \(eds\) Modern global economic system: evolutional development vs. revolutionary leap. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 1125–1132")), and the slow decision-making process globally (Hars, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR144 "Hars A \(2022\) AI and international law—legal personality and avenues for regulation. Hung J Leg Stud 62:320–344. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1556/2052.2022.00352
                  
                ")). Doing so is challenging given the push to assume leadership at the domestic level (Ala-Pietilä and Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR5 "Ala-Pietilä P, Smuha NA \(2021\) A framework for global cooperation on artificial intelligence and its governance. In Reflections on artificial intelligence for humanity. Springer, Dordrecht")). The EU proposed through its various groups different suggestions for the regulation of this field. Meanwhile, Beijing suggested specific principles considering the priorities of Chinese government such as international competition and economic development. The US initiated a similar process while seeking to maintain its leadership. Meanwhile, many nations lack the technological and financial capacity to participate in this debate (Carrillo, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR51 "Carrillo MR \(2020\) Artificial intelligence: from ethics to law. Telecom Policy 44:101937. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.telpol.2020.101937
                  
                ")). Besides the ones mentioned, those active include Canada, France, Germany, India, Israel, Japan, Russia, South Korea, and the United Kingdom. Questions to whether all these nations can collaborate through legal mechanisms creating trust; confidence and assigning responsibility remain unanswered (Gill, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR126 "Gill AS \(2019\) Artificial intelligence and international security: the long view. Ethics Int Aff 33:169–179. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/S0892679419000145
                  
                "); Boutin, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR35 "Boutin B \(2022\) State responsibility in relation to military applications of artificial intelligence. LJIL 36:133–150. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/S0922156522000607
                  
                ")), due to the existence of diverging interests (Blodgett-Ford, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR31 "Blodgett-Ford SJ \(2018\) Future privacy: a real right to privacy for artificial intelligence. In Research handbook on the law of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham, pp 307–352")), as these nations are making huge investments in the various aspects related to this field (Winter, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR345 "Winter E \(2022\) The compatibility of autonomous weapons with the principles of international humanitarian law. J Confl Secur Law 27:1–20. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/jcsl/krac001
                  
                
                  https://opendocs.ids.ac.uk/opendocs/bitstream/handle/20.500.12413/14252/448_Intersections_of_Technology_and_Civil_Society.pdf?sequence=39&isAllowed=y
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Overall, states at this stage are seeking to adopt a holistic regulatory approach to this topic and attempting to reflect such strategy in the global sphere (Roberts et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR256 "Roberts H et al. \(2021\) The Chinese approach to artificial intelligence: an analysis of policy, ethics, and regulation. AI Soc 36:59–77. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-020-00992-2
                  
                ")). The difference in the various visions among governments is complicating the dialog and attempt to adopt proper AI regime (Cath et al., [2018b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR54 "Cath C et al. \(2018b\) Artificial intelligence and the ‘good society’: the US, EU, and UK approach. Sci Eng Ethics 24:505–528. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11948-017-9901-7
                  
                ")), as national administrations are themselves experimenting with AI systems (Surden, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR288 "Surden H \(2019\) Artificial intelligence and law: an overview. GA State Univ Law Rev 35:1305–1337. 
                  https://scholar.law.colorado.edu/faculty-articles/1234
                  
                ")). This is not say that it is an easy task for nations to address given the numerous aspects that must be dealt with (Akkus, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR16 "Akkus B \(2023\) An assessment of the acceptance of meaningful human control as a norm of international law in armed conflicts using artificial intelligence. J Ank Bar Assoc 81:49–102. 
                  https://doi.org/10.30915/abd.1143722
                  
                ")).

Given all the above, little progress has been made on this front (Haner and Garcia, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR143 "Haner J, Garcia D \(2019\) The artificial intelligence arms race: Trends and world leaders in autonomous weapons development. Glob Policy 10:331–337. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1758-5899.12713
                  
                ")) signaling the need to further develop the domestic rules (Gerke et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR121 "Gerke S, Minssen T, Cohen G \(2020\) Ethical and legal challenges of artificial intelligence-driven healthcare. Artif Intell Health Care. 295-336. 10.1016%2FB978-0-12-818438-7.00012-5")). It is in this context that some call the scientific community to guide states in their endeavor to prevent the occurrence of harm and establish adequate responsibility and accountability mechanisms (Garcia, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR115 "Garcia D \(2016\) Future arms, technologies, and international law: preventive security governance. EJIS 1:94–111. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/eis.2015.7
                  
                ")). This is as neither China nor the US appears to favor establishing a regulatory authority, preferring instead to give their companies a competitive edge in the global race to develop AI. Without their support, such an authority would be ineffective in assessing the human rights, ethical, and societal implications of cutting-edge AI technology (Whyman, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR343 "Whyman B \(2023\) AI regulation is coming—what is the likely outcome? Center for Strategic & International Studies. 
                  https://www.csis.org/blogs/strategic-technologies-blog/ai-regulation-coming-what-likely-outcome
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); Sullivan, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR283 "Sullivan M \(2023\) Global AI regulation: a closer look at the US, EU, and China. 
                  https://transcend.io/blog/ai-regulation#united-states
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). However, both nations cannot afford to be excluded globally. Therefore, they are participating in international discussions on AI regulation to influence its development (Smuha, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR277 "Smuha NA \(2023\) Biden, Bletchley, and the emerging international law of AI. Verfassungsblog. 
                  https://verfassungsblog.de/biden-bletchley-and-the-emerging-international-law-of-ai/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); Lee, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR192 "Lee R \(2024\) Global AI regulation efforts prompt US-China talks. Tech Informed. 
                  https://techinformed.com/global-ai-regulation-efforts-prompt-us-china-talks/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

### Natural persons

In international public law, individuals are usually not considered subjects that can influence the shaping of regimes as this field organizes state to state relations via agreements and nonbinding instruments (Von Bogdandy et al., [2017](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR333 "Von Bogdandy A, Goldmann M, Venzke I \(2017\) From public international to international public law: translating world public opinion into international public authority. Eur J Int Law 28:115–145. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ejil/chx002
                  
                ")). This is why, citizens were not added to the conceptual framework established by the authors. Yet, AI technologies affect the lives of citizens (Ala-Pietilä and Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR5 "Ala-Pietilä P, Smuha NA \(2021\) A framework for global cooperation on artificial intelligence and its governance. In Reflections on artificial intelligence for humanity. Springer, Dordrecht"); Strange and Tucker, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR286 "Strange M, Tucker J \(2023\) Global governance and the normalization of artificial intelligence as ‘good’ for human health. AI Soc. 1–10. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-023-01774-2
                  
                ")). The law applicable to natural persons in this context is expected to be affected (Liu and Lin, [2020b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR202 "Liu HW, Lin CF \(2020b\) Artificial intelligence and global trade governance: a pluralist agenda. Harv Int Law J 61:407–450")), at the various levels especially considering the regimes in place such as democracy and dictatorships (Wright, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR353 "Wright N \(2018\) How artificial intelligence will reshape the global order. Foreign Aff. 
                  https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/world/2018-07-10/how-artificial-intelligence-will-reshape-global-order
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). The relevance of this field was seen during Covid19 pandemic as people’s vaccination status was tracked digitally across borders (Zeng, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR360 "Zeng J \(2020\) Artificial intelligence and China’s authoritarian governance. Int Aff 96:1441–1459. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ia/iiaa172
                  
                ")), while the use of AI for predicting behavioral patterns is currently occurring (Saurae et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR265 "Saura JR, Ribeiro-Soriano D, Palacios-Marques D \(2022\) Assessing behavioral data science privacy issues in government artificial intelligence deployment. Govern Info Q 39:101679. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2022.101679
                  
                ")). Simultaneously, AI further enhanced citizens participation in the decision-making process through citizens’ science (Dauvergne, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR79 "Dauvergne P \(2021\) The globalization of artificial intelligence: consequences for the politics of environmentalism. Globalizations 18:285–299. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/14747731.2020.1785670
                  
                ")), and other means as calls for their involvement is taking place (Büthe et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR49 "Buthe T et al. \(2022\) Governing AI—attempting to herd cats? Introduction to the special issue on the governance of artificial intelligence. J Eur Public Policy 29:1721–1752. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/13501763.2022.2126515
                  
                ")), given their contribution to human survival as witnessed during Covid19 (Mavridis et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR213 "Mavridis N, Quack E, Wussow K \(2021\) Leveraging artificial intelligence and digital tech to help citizens, societies, and economies survive and strive during pandemics. In: Gruenwald L, Jain S, Groppe S \(eds\) Leveraging artificial intelligence in global epidemics. Elsevier, Amsterdam, pp 29–69")).

Hence, citizens protection is needed in the various strategies used as awareness of AI risks is increasing and as their behavior is impacted by new technologies requiring legal clarity. Citizens expect cooperation transnationally to guarantee benefits and reduce risks (Ala-Pietilä and Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR5 "Ala-Pietilä P, Smuha NA \(2021\) A framework for global cooperation on artificial intelligence and its governance. In Reflections on artificial intelligence for humanity. Springer, Dordrecht")), as global governance via law is impacting their lives and communities (Benvenisti, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR27 "Benvenisti E \(2018\) Upholding democracy amid the challenges of new technology: What role for the law of global governance? EJIL 29:9–82. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ejil/chy013
                  
                ")), and as they are progressively assuming the role of responsible global actors (How, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR154 "How ML \(2021\) Artificial intelligence for social good in responsible global citizenship education: an inclusive democratized low-code approach. In Proceedings of the 2nd world conference on teaching and education, Vienna, Austria")), via their progressive engagement (Sharma et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR273 "Sharma GD, Yadav A, Chopra R \(2020\) Artificial intelligence and effective governance: a review, critique and research agenda. Sustain Futures 2:100004. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.sftr.2019.100004
                  
                ")), at the different levels (Medaglia et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR220 "Medaglia R, Gil-Garcia R, Pardo TA \(2021\) Artificial intelligence in government: taking stock and moving forward. Soc Sci Comput Rev 41:123–140. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/08944393211034087
                  
                ")).

This is not to say that citizens’ participation in law-making globally is an easy task as the complexity of the issues discussed including AI may in many instances be beyond the capacity of numerous people to assess (Benvenisti, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR27 "Benvenisti E \(2018\) Upholding democracy amid the challenges of new technology: What role for the law of global governance? EJIL 29:9–82. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ejil/chy013
                  
                ")). Indeed, this technology’s value and relevance within different sectors remains poorly understood (Wang et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR338 "Wang C, Teo TSH, Janssen M \(2021\) Public and private value creation using artificial intelligence: an empirical study of AI voice robot users in Chinese public sector. Int J Inf Manag 61:102401. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijinfomgt.2021.102401
                  
                ")), since many lack a proper knowledge of its mode of functioning (Robinson, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR257 "Robinson SC \(2020\) Trust, transparency, and openness: how inclusion of cultural values shapes Nordic national public policy strategies for artificial intelligence \(AI\). 63: 101421. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.techsoc.2020.101421
                  
                ")). Still, gaining the population’s approval provides great legitimacy to the adoption and implementation international rules in this regard (Gesk and Leyer, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR123 "Gesk TS, Leyer M \(2022\) Artificial Intelligence in public services: when and why citizens accept its usage. Gov Inf Q 39:101704. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2022.101704
                  
                ")), as trust in such technology is lacking given the existing risks (Konig, [2023a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR180 "Konig PD \(2023a\) Citizen conceptions of democracy and support for artificial intelligence in government and politics. ECPR 62:1280–1300. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1475-6765.12570
                  
                ")). To tackle this issue, regulatory intervention is conceived as the way forward to calm the concerns (Konig et al., [2023b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR181 "Konig PD, Wurster S, Siewert MB \(2023b\) Sustainability challenges of artificial intelligence and Citizens’ regulatory preferences. Govern Inf Q 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2023.101863
                  
                ")), and ensure the overall population satisfaction (Chatterjee et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR58 "Chatterjee S, Khorana S, Kizgin H \(2022\) Harnessing the potential of artificial intelligence to foster citizens satisfaction: an empirical study on India. Gov Inf Q 39:101621. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2021.101621
                  
                ")), considering their perception of the rules in place for governing AI (Starke and Lunich, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR282 "Starke C, Lunich M \(2020\) Artificial intelligence for political decision-making in the European Union: effects on citizens’ perceptions of input, throughput, and output legitimacy. Data Policy 2:e16-1–e16-17. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/dap.2020.19
                  
                ")), and their overall expectations (Wirtz et al., [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR346 "Wirtz BW, Weyerer JC, Geyer C \(2019\) Artificial intelligence and the public sector—applications and challenges. Int J Public Adm 42:596–615. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/01900692.2018.1498103
                  
                ")), with regards to being responsive to fulfilling their needs (Valle-Cruz et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR318 "Valle-Cruz D et al. \(2020\) Assessing the public policy-cycle framework in the age of artificial intelligence: from agenda-setting to policy evaluation. Govern Inf Q 37:101509. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2020.101509
                  
                ")), based on different benchmarks such as ethics (Kleizen et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR178 "Kleizen B, Van Dooren W, Verhoest K, Tan E \(2023\) Do citizens trust trustworthy artificial intelligence? Experimental evidence on the limits of ethical AI measures in government. Govern Inf Q 101834. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2023.101834
                  
                ")).

This begs the question of whether it is time for citizens to play an active role in lawmaking globally. This issue has been the subject of debate for a while now not only concerning AI regulation but also other fields being tackled transnationally (Sohn, [1983](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR278 "Sohn LB \(1983\) The new international law: protection of the rights of individuals rather than states. Am Univ Law Rev 32:1"); Hollis, [2002](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR151 "Hollis DB \(2002\) Private actors in public international law: Amicus curiae and the case for the retention of state sovereignty. Boston Coll Int Comp Law Rev 25:235")). One can argue that this role is being played indirectly via civil society organizations that are increasingly impacting the international discourse, and as citizens actions tend to be unorganized (Buhmann and Fieseler, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR40 "Buhmann A, Fieseler C \(2021\) Towards a deliberative framework for responsible innovation in artificial intelligence. Technol Soc J 64:101475. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.techsoc.2020.101475
                  
                ")). One may also argue that states assume similar responsibility when participating in international lawmaking due to its responsibility of protecting its own citizens (Feijoo et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR103 "Feijoo C et al. \(2020\) Harnessing artificial intelligence \(AI\) to increase wellbeing for all: the case for a new technology diplomacy. Telecom Policy 44:101988. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.telpol.2020.101988
                  
                ")). It is worth mentioning that similar situation is encountered domestically where a clash is happening since many states de facto take decisions in the name of collective national interests. The message overall is the need to earn citizens trust via a responsible and trustworthy governance frameworks (Isom, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR162 "Isom P \(2022\) Earning citizen confidence through a comprehensive approach to responsible and trustworthy AI stewardship and governance. J AI Robot Workplace Autom 1:278–284")), that serves their interests (Yigitcanlar, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR357 "Yigitcanlar T \(2020\) Artificial intelligence technologies and related urban planning and development concepts: how are they perceived and utilized in Australia? J Open Innov Technol Mark Complex 6:187. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3390/joitmc6040187
                  
                ")). Otherwise, such trust can be reduced and even lost due to a variety of reasons such as lack of fairness, absence of transparency (Zuiderwijk et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR363 "Zuiderwijk A, Chen YC, Salem F \(2021\) Implications of the use of artificial intelligence in public governance: a systematic literature review and a research agenda. Govern Inf Q 38:101577. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2021.101577
                  
                ")), and lack of freedom (Al Shebli et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR11 "Al Shebli K et al. \(2021\) RTA’s employees perceptions toward the efficiency of artificial intelligence and big data utilization in providing smart services to the residents of Dubai. In: Hassanien AE, et al., \(eds\) Proceedings of the international conference on artificial intelligence and computer vision. Springer, Dordrecht")). This is very important when one considers the negative consequences of AI witnessed so far (Wirtz et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR347 "Wirtz BW, Weyerer JC, Sturm BJ \(2020\) The dark sides of artificial intelligence: an integrated AI governance framework for public administration. Int J Public Adm 43:818–829. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/01900692.2020.1749851
                  
                ")).

### Civil society organizations

Social institutions outside of the state that are selfgoverned, voluntary and not-for-profit have been pointing out to shortcomings and failures of policies at the national and international level. These include those related to culture and recreation; education and research; health; social services; environment; development and housing; law, advocacy, and politics; religion etc (Lynn et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR204 "Lynn T et al. \(2022\) Digital towns: accelerating and measuring the digital transformation of rural societies and economies. Springer, Dordrecht")). Activists and social movements are accepted as part of the international community affecting lawmaking (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")), given their importance to the advancement of human well-being (Mohammed et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR225 "Mohammed O et al. \(2022\) Data4Good: an established framework for supporting civil society organizations. 2022 IEEE International Humanitarian Technology Conference \(IHTC\), Ottawa, ON, Canada")). Various terminologies were established to refer to them such as international civil society; transnational social movements; global social-change organizations; world civic politics and others (O’Brien, [2005](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR233 "O’Brien R \(2005\) Global civil society and global governance. In: Ba AD, Hoffman MJ \(eds\) Contending perspectives on global governance: coherence and contestation. Routledge, Abingdon")). The latter witnessed a vast expansion in the last decades (Jacobs, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR165 "Jacobs G \(2018\) Cryptocurrencies & the challenge of global governance. Cadmus 3:109–123")). Over the years they expressed concerns about AI and have conducted multiple studies in relation to this field (Zhang et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR362 "Zhang B et al. \(2021\) Ethics and governance of artificial intelligence: evidence from a survey of machine learning researchers. J Artif Intell Res 71:591–666")), where the law is needed to address any of the potential shortcomings (Fay, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR102 "Fay R \(2019\) Digital platforms require a global governance framework. Centre for International Governance Innovation. 
                  https://www.cigionline.org/articles/digital-platforms-require-global-governance-framework/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). The debate concerning this matter is polarized due to the pros and cons of digitalization (Sundberg, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR287 "Sundberg L \(2023\) Towards the digital risk society: a review. Hum Aff 1–14. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1515/humaff-2023-0057
                  
                ")). Still, the involvement of this specific actor is crucial given its unique position (Jones, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR169 "Jones M \(2022\) Civil society and AI: striving for ethical governance. Alexander von Humboldt. 
                  https://www.hiig.de/en/civil-society-and-ai-striving-for-ethical-governance/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")), which is why many are addressing the relevance of such participation (Furendal, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR110 "Furendal M \(2023\) Non-state actors and the global governance of artificial intelligence. ECPR. 
                  https://ecpr.eu/Events/Event/PaperDetails/69246
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

The main aim of civil society organizations is the protection of human rights from breaches that may occur because of the use of AI (Ozdan, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR238 "Ozdan S \(2023\) Don’t leave artificial intelligence alone: It could hurt human rights. In: Jandric P, et al., \(eds\) Constructing postdigital research: method and rmancipation. Springer, Dordrecht")), where the implications of the use of such technologies are being examined (Maxwell and Tomlinson, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR215 "Maxwell J, Tomlinson J \(2020\) Public law and technology: mapping and Analysing legal responses in UK civil society. Judic Rev 25:28–38. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/10854681.2020.1732741
                  
                ")). The end goal is avoiding the potential negative consequences on populations and communities through transnational efforts (WHO, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR351 "World Health Organization \(2021\) Ethics and governance of artificial intelligence for health. 
                  https://www.who.int/publications/i/item/9789240029200
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). They are seen as means to critically assess technological progress. These are important public stakeholders with whom to collaborate. Their role lies in agenda setting, consultation and discussions impacting the adoption of transnational laws and their implementation by state authorities domestically (Buiten et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR42 "Buiten M, de Streel A, Peitz M \(2023\) The law and economics of AI liability. Comput Law Secur Rev 48:105794. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.clsr.2023.105794
                  
                "); Hoffman-Riem [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR367 "Hoffman-Riem W \(2020\) Artificial intelligence as a challenge for law and regulation. In: Wischmeyer T, Rademacher T \(eds\) Regulating artificial intelligence. Springer, Dordrecht, pp 1–29")). For instance, these entities take part in the AI for Good summit platform (Jelinek et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR166 "Jelinek T, Wallach W, Kerimi D \(2021\) Policy brief: the creation of a G20 coordinating committee for the governance of artificial intelligence. AI Ethics 1:141–150. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43681-020-00019-y
                  
                "); Butcher and Beridze, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR47 "Butcher JG, Beridze I \(2019\) What is the state of artificial intelligence governance globally? RUSI 164:88–96. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694260
                  
                ")). They also played a role regionally in the negotiations and formulation of AI policies most recently the EU AI Act (Tallberg et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR290 "Tallberg et al. \(2023\) The global governance of artificial intelligence: next steps for empirical and normative research. Rev Int Stud 25:1–23. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/isr/viad040
                  
                ")). Notably, there are initiatives to bring together states, companies, academia, and NGOs, such as the Athens Roundtable by the Future Society despite the high-costs associated with participation (The Future Society, [n.d.](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR296 "The Future Society \(n.d.\) The Athens Roundtable. 
                  https://thefuturesociety.org/workstream/the-athens-roundtable-on-ai-and-the-rule-of-law/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). It remains true that even on such platforms, only a few states, companies, NGOs, and academics can participate although broader cooperation is needed.

Globally, civil society organizations call strongly for the regulation of this field (Martin and Freeland, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR211 "Martin AS, Freeland S \(2021\) The advent of artificial intelligence in space activities: new legal challenges. Space Policy 55:101408. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.spacepol.2020.101408
                  
                ")), while highlighting the dilemmas to decisionmakers and stakeholders with the objective of effectively governing this sector (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")), and opposing its use in situations that might entail risks (Lewis, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR196 "Lewis L \(2019\) Resolving the battle over artificial intelligence in war. RUSI J 164:62–71. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694228
                  
                ")). For instance, they called for the adoption of a binding instrument prohibiting the use of AI in lethal autonomous weapons systems (Schmitt, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR268 "Schmitt L \(2022\) Mapping global AI governance: a nascent regime in a fragmented landscape. AI Ethics 2:303–314. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43681-021-00083-y
                  
                ")). Additionally, they developed ethical principles and guidelines paving the road ahead of legislators domestically and internationally (Koniakou, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR179 "Koniakou V \(2023\) From the “rush to ethics” to the “race for governance” in artificial intelligence. Inf Syst Front 25:71–102. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10796-022-10300-6
                  
                ")). Examples include non-legally binding instruments called “Internet Bills of Rights” having different principles and standards (Celeste et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR59 "Celeste E et al. \(2023\) The content governance dilemma: digital constitutionalism, social media and the search for a global standard. Springer, Dordrecht")). The latters may try to develop a specific set of values that are ethically acceptable (Von Ingersleben-Seip [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR372 "Von Ingersleben-Seip N \(2023\) Competition and cooperation in artificial intelligence standard setting: explaining emergent patterns. Rev Policy Res 40:781–810. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1111/ropr.12538
                  
                ")). These have been criticized by some entities as a means to escape from regulation or delay it in the name of ethics (van Dijk et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR321 "Van Dijk N, Casiraghi S, Gutwirth S \(2021\) The ‘ethification’ of ICT governance. Artificial intelligence and data protection in the European Union. Comput Law Secur Rev 43:105597. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.clsr.2021.105597
                  
                ")). Some scholars established a framework based on which AI can be used to empower civil society (Savaget et al., [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR266 "Savaget P, Chiarini T, Evans S \(2019\) Empowering political participation through artificial intelligence. Sci Public Policy 46:369–380. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/scipol/scy064
                  
                ")). As a non-state actor, AI may also lead to a shift in power from states towards these organizations (Maas, [2019a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR205 "Maas M \(2019a\) International law does not compute: artificial intelligence and the development, displacement or destruction of the global legal order. Melb J Int Law 29:1–29")), although states will always remain the more powerful. In fact, it has been noticed that it is not always possible for this actor to influence the development of rules in this regard. On the contrary, this technology will disrupt the traditional role of civil society (Williams, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR344 "Williams KC \(2018\) Intersections of technology and civil society. Knowledge, evidence and learning for development")), that remains despite this considered a partner in the development of the global AI legal architecture (Walz and Firth-Butterfield, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR337 "Walz A, Firth-Butterfield K \(2019\) Implementing ethics into artificial intelligence: a contribution, from a legal perspective, to the development of an AI governance regime. Duke Law Technol Rev 18:176–231. 
                  https://scholarship.law.duke.edu/dltr/vol18/iss1/17
                  
                ")), to ensure the effectiveness of the rules to be adopted (Korinek and Stiglitz, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR183 "Korinek A, Stiglitz JE \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, globalization, and strategies for economic development. Centre for the Governance of AI. 
                  https://www.governance.ai/research-paper/artificial-intelligence-globalization-and-strategies-for-economic-development
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). In some instances, civil society was not up to date concerning new innovations (Kostantinova et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR182 "Konstantinova Y, et al. \(2020\) State and civil society in the digital age: a search for methodological approaches. In Proceedings of the innovations in digital economy: second international scientific conference, SPBPU IDE 2020, St. Petersburg, Russia, October 22–23, 2020, revised selected papers 15:1–7. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1145/3444465.3444511
                  
                ")). Meanwhile, the high costs of participation may affect such entities presence in the debate (Cihon et al., [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR68 "Cihon P, Maas MM, Kemp L \(2020a\) Should artificial intelligence governance be centralised? Design lessons from history. AIES. 228–234. 
                  https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2001.03573
                  
                ")). Teaming up with tech companies is taking place to address some of the abovementioned issues (Latonero, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR190 "Latonero M \(2018\) Governing artificial intelligence: upholding human rights & dignity. Data & Society. 
                  https://datasociety.net/library/governing-artificial-intelligence/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

### Corporate entities and business enterprise

Multinational corporations are conducting businesses everywhere rendering the borders obsolete (Kelly and Moreno-Ocampo, [2016](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR171 "Kelly MJ, Moreno-Ocampo L \(2016\) Prosecuting corporations for genocide. Oxford University Press, Oxford")). They are increasingly influencing the creation, implementation, and enforcement of international law (Butler, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR48 "Butler J \(2020\) The corporate keepers of international law. AJIL 114:189–220. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/ajil.2020.1
                  
                ")). Simultaneously, they are subjects of this regulatory framework due to their transboundary impact which raises questions such as those related to liability and compensation (Kinley and Tadaki, [2004](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR175 "Kinley D, Tadaki J \(2004\) From talk to walk: The emergence of human rights responsibilities for corporations at international law. Virginia J Int Law 44:931")), for damages including human rights violations (Kinley and Chambers, [2006](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR176 "Kinley D, Chambers R \(2006\) The UN human rights norms for corporations: the private implications of public international law. Hum Rights Law Rev 6:447–497. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/hrlr/ngl020
                  
                ")). The nature of their obligations is widely debated where suggestions are made to ensure they are held accountable. Their conduct is being addressed indirectly by asking states to adopt rules applicable to the corporate sector with few exceptions like in the cases of war crimes and forced labor where international norms are in place (Vazquez, [2005](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR322 "Vazquez CM \(2005\) Direct vs. Indirect obligations of corporations under international law. Colum J Transnatl L 43:927–959. 
                  https://scholarship.law.georgetown.edu/facpub/980
                  
                ")).

Companies are using AI in various sectors such as finance and insurance; retail, healthcare, and information services where huge investments are made (Jablonowska et al., [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR163 "Jablonowska A et al. \(2018\) Consumer law and artificial intelligence challenges to the EU consumer law and policy stemming from the business’ use of artificial intelligence. European University Institute Working paper. 
                  https://cadmus.eui.eu/handle/1814/57484
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Companies play a vital role in the development of AI nationally and internationally as their products cross national borders where rules vary (Latonero, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR190 "Latonero M \(2018\) Governing artificial intelligence: upholding human rights & dignity. Data & Society. 
                  https://datasociety.net/library/governing-artificial-intelligence/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). They assume a great responsibility when establishing and using such technology (Kriebitz and Lutge, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR185 "Kriebitz A, Lutge C \(2020\) Artificial intelligence and human rights: a business ethical assessment. Bus Hum Rights J 5:84–104. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/bhj.2019.28
                  
                ")). This includes traditional technology companies and emerging ones (Lin, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR198 "Lin TCW \(2019\) Artificial intelligence, finance, and the law. Fordham Law Rev 88:531–551. 
                  https://ir.lawnet.fordham.edu/flr/vol88/iss2/6
                  
                ")). The costs of moving the AI resources of a company be it talent, capital or infrastructure globally are high especially given the existence of legal obstacles as a result of entering new jurisdictions (Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR276 "Smuha NA \(2021\) From a ‘race to AI’ to a ‘race to AI regulation’: regulatory competition for artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 13:57–84. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2021.1898300
                  
                ")). The latter raise concerns concerning the possible oversight of these stakeholders given their great technical infrastructures and know-how (Stahl et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR280 "Stahl BC et al. \(2021\) Artificial intelligence for human flourishing—beyond principles for machine learning. J Bus Res 124:374–388. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbusres.2020.11.030
                  
                ")), and the overall influence of this technology on society if left unchecked (Leslie et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR194 "Leslie D et al. \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, human rights, democracy, and the rule of law—a primer. Council of Europe. 
                  https://edoc.coe.int/en/artificial-intelligence/10206-artificial-intelligence-human-rights-democracy-and-the-rule-of-law-a-primer.html
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Therefore, regulating AI activities seems the way forward where companies are subject to these rules (Bennett, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR25 "Bennett M \(2023\) AI regulation: What businesses need to know. TechTarget. 
                  https://www.techtarget.com/searchenterpriseai/feature/AI-regulation-What-businesses-need-to-know
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

Paving the road in this sector meant that corporations pay great attention to regulations (Cath, [2018a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR53 "Cath C \(2018a\) Governing artificial intelligence: ethical, legal and technical opportunities and challenges. Philos Trans R Soc A 376:1–8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1098/rsta.2018.0080
                  
                ")), given their interests in profits and protecting their investments (Hars, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR144 "Hars A \(2022\) AI and international law—legal personality and avenues for regulation. Hung J Leg Stud 62:320–344. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1556/2052.2022.00352
                  
                ")). This is why, it is being said that corporations are not scared of regulations but the type and content of those (Chou, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR66 "Chou D \(2023\) AI companies aren’t afraid of regulation—we want it to be international and inclusive. The Guardian. 
                  https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/aug/04/ai-companies-regulation-international-inclusive
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). A corporation’s product need to comply with laws applicable to AI like data protection and intellectual property. They also need to adapt to new laws associated with this field. In both cases, the goal is to reduce or avoid risks emerging from non-compliance and strengthening their trust and reputation among the stakeholders. Companies involvement is needed to also ensure that innovation and competitiveness are not hampered by laws where the latters should rather facilitate this reality. This can happen via the creation of standards and guidelines on AI use (Holitsche [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR373 "Holitsche S \(2023\) How companies can contribute to the development of international law frameworks for AI regulation. Linkedin. 
                  https://www.linkedin.com/pulse/how-companies-can-contribute-development-law-ai-stefan-holitschke/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Already, for instance, some companies raised concerns with regards to the EU’s AI law proposal for potentially affecting competitiveness and resulting in investments fleeing the European market (Wodecki, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR349 "Wodecki B \(2023\) Top European companies: ‘Serious concerns’ about EU AI act. AI Bus. 
                  https://aibusiness.com/nlp/top-european-companies-serious-concerns-about-eu-ai-act
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Finally, corporations have a leadershing role to play by applying ethical and responsible AI practices, endorsing global instruments providing frameworks for addressing this technology’s activities (Holitsche [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR373 "Holitsche S \(2023\) How companies can contribute to the development of international law frameworks for AI regulation. Linkedin. 
                  https://www.linkedin.com/pulse/how-companies-can-contribute-development-law-ai-stefan-holitschke/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Companies like Google and Amazon had made commitments to make sure that AI products and systems are safe and trustworthy (Diamond, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR85 "Diamond J \(2023\) Leading AI companies commit to outside testing of AI systems and other safety commitments. CNN Politics. 
                  https://edition.cnn.com/2023/07/21/politics/white-house-artificial-intelligence/index.html
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

The private sector is calling for the adoption of soft law mechanisms to protect their interests. Many businesses created principles and standards. In contrast, hard laws consider the interests of various stakeholders including governments and the public in an unpredictable process (Gutierrez et al., [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR136 "Gutierrez CI, Marchant G, Tournas L \(2020\) Lessons for artificial intelligence from historical uses of soft law governance. Jurismetrics 61:133–149")). For instance, IBM and Microsoft made a commitment to the Vatican to safeguard humankind in the development of AI in the Rome Call for AI Ethics (Hickman and Petrin, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR148 "Hickman E, Petrin M \(2021\) Trustworthy AI and corporate governance: The EU’s ethics guidelines for trustworthy artificial intelligence from a company law perspective. Eur Bus Organ Law Rev 22:593–625. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s40804-021-00224-0
                  
                ")). Various mechanisms have been proposed to tackle transnational companies use of AI. These include conditional support from international organizations such as the World Bank; the adoption of a global corporation treaty stipulating the responsibilities of AI use creating obligations for companies having a state’s nationality and having periodic monitoring (Schwarz, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR271 "Schwarz EC \(2019\) Human vs. machine: a framework of responsibilities and duties of transnational corporations for respecting human rights in the use of artificial intelligence. Colum J Transnatl Law 58:232")). More concretely, there are calls for governments to establish global regulatory frameworks where private sector entities compete to achieve specific legal objectives at both local and international levels (Clark and Hadfield, [2019a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR70 "Clark J, Hadfield GK \(2019a\) Regulatory markets for AI safety. Comput Soc. 1–23. 
                  https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2001.00078
                  
                ")). These markets are designed to address uncertainty more efficiently than direct regulations (Bova et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR36 "Bova P, Di Stefano A, Han TH \(2023\) Both eyes open: Vigilant incentives help regulatory markets improve AI safety. Artif Intell. 1–42. 
                  https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2303.03174
                  
                ")) as legislators often lack the necessary technical knowledge, whereas market forces and industry are better equipped to drive the adoption of appropriate regulations (Hadfield and Clark, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR138 "Hadfield GK, Clark J \(2023\) Regulatory markets: the future of AI governance. Artif Intell 1–29. 
                  https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2304.04914
                  
                ")). It is worth mentioning that some caution of companies who are already a monopoly in the digital technology business having a great infrastructural importance (Peng et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR246 "Peng SY, Lin CF, Streinz T \(2021\) Artificial intelligence and international economic law: disruption, regulation and reconfiguration. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge")). Meanwhile, organizations like the UN are calling corporations to live up to their responsibilities concerning this field (Turk, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR302 "Turk V \(2023\) Artificial intelligence must be grounded in human rights, says High Commissioner. 
                  https://www.ohchr.org/en/statements/2023/07/artificial-intelligence-must-be-grounded-human-rights-says-high-commissioner
                  
                ")). The challenge is to ensure that companies still move fast despite the regulations being drafted (Thomson Reuters [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR374 "Thomson Reuters \(2023\) Can AI companies still move fast and break things despite pending regulations? 
                  https://www.thomsonreuters.com/en-us/posts/government/ai-pending-regulations/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

### International organizations

Transnational entities such as the UN and the World Trade Organization produce international laws in various forms including binding, customary, and soft rules (Dung and Sartor, [2011](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR93 "Dung PM, Sartor G \(2011\) The modular logic of private international law. Artif Intell Law 19:233–261. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10506-011-9112-5
                  
                ")). These initially especially after World War II were heavily trusted by the different stakeholders as the gatekeepers of peace, security, and prosperity. Only in later years, criticisms started emerging concerning their impact (Benvenisti, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR27 "Benvenisti E \(2018\) Upholding democracy amid the challenges of new technology: What role for the law of global governance? EJIL 29:9–82. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1093/ejil/chy013
                  
                ")). Still, their role being increasingly important since then and after the end of the cold war (Alvarez, [2006](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR14 "Alvarez JE \(2006\) International organizations as law-makers. Oxford University Press, Oxford")).

Various global organizations are creating AI frameworks especially in the last decade (Johnson and Bowman, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR167 "Johnson WG, Bowman DM \(2021\) A survey of instruments and institutions available for the global governance of artificial intelligence. IEEE Technol Soc Mag 40:68–76. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1109/MTS.2021.3123745
                  
                ")), or working on rules associated with this field (Roumate, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR259 "Roumate F \(2021\) Artificial intelligence, ethics and international human rights law. IRIE 29:1–10")). These include the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development; the International Organization for Standardization (Martin and Freeland, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR211 "Martin AS, Freeland S \(2021\) The advent of artificial intelligence in space activities: new legal challenges. Space Policy 55:101408. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.spacepol.2020.101408
                  
                ")); the Institute for Electrical and Electronics Engineers; Centre for the Study of Existential Risks, Future of Humanity Institute, Future of Life Institute, Future Society, Leverhulme Centre for the Future of Intelligence; AI Now Institute; the Machine Intelligence Research Institute and the Partnership for AI. Many UN organizations are also dealing with topic such as High-Level Panel on Digital Cooperation; UN Interregional Crime and Justice Research Institute; International Telecommunication Union; UN University Center for Policy Research; International Labour Organization and UN Office for Disarmament Affairs (Butcher and Beridze, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR47 "Butcher JG, Beridze I \(2019\) What is the state of artificial intelligence governance globally? RUSI 164:88–96. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694260
                  
                ")). Only major international organizations with a distinct legal personality and a founding international treaty (such as UNESCO, EU and the Council of Europe) will serve as platforms for international cooperation.

All these deal with one or several aspects in relation to AI governance and regulations asking questions such as what is the best approach to regulate this sector. Issues to address inter alia risks, challenges, deployment, harmonization, national implementation (Butcher and Beridze, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR47 "Butcher JG, Beridze I \(2019\) What is the state of artificial intelligence governance globally? RUSI 164:88–96. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/03071847.2019.1694260
                  
                "); Finocchiaro, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR105 "Finocchiaro G \(2023\) The regulation of artificial intelligence. AI & Society. 1–8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00146-023-01650-z
                  
                ")). Their initiatives and efforts often make an impact on the law-making process globally (Velasco, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR324 "Velasco C \(2022\) Cybercrime and artificial intelligence. An overview of the work of international organizations on criminal justice and the international applicable instruments. ERA Forum 23:109–126. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s12027-022-00702-z
                  
                ")), especially with the adoption of nonbinding instruments and declarations containing for instance ethical principles (Taddeo et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR289 "Taddeo M et al. \(2021\) Ethical principles for artificial intelligence in national defence. Philos Technol 34:1707–1729. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s13347-021-00482-3
                  
                ")). The goal is to create consensus among nations parties to these organizations on regulatory AI elements without for the time having binding obligations upon states or being able to intervene in domestic decisions in relation to this topic (Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR276 "Smuha NA \(2021\) From a ‘race to AI’ to a ‘race to AI regulation’: regulatory competition for artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 13:57–84. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2021.1898300
                  
                ")). Doing so is very challenging due to the different views held by the members concerning regulations (Deeks, [2020b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR83 "Deeks A \(2020b\) Introduction to the symposium: How will artificial intelligence affect international law? AJIL 114:138–140. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1017/aju.2020.29
                  
                ")). Yet, cooperation is the way forward considering the nature of the digital world that has a global reach (Algorithms, Artificial Intelligence and the Law, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR375 "Algorithms, Artificial Intelligence and the Law \(2019\) The Sir Henry Brooke Lecture for BAILII Freshfields Bruckhaus Deringer, London; Lord Sales, Justice of the UK Supreme Court 12 November 2019")), where transnational entities are seen as a means to strengthen collaboration (Kerry et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR172 "Kerry CF et al. \(2021\) Strengthening international cooperation on AI. 
                  https://www.brookings.edu/wp-content/uploads/2021/10/Strengthening-International-Cooperation-AI_Oct21.pdf
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). International organizations create a space for states and other stakeholders to adopt AI norms and attempt to facilitate the reaching of an agreement among the parties (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")).

Given that the work of the various entities is not connected, suggestions are made for the creation of instruments for the coordination of activities of international organizations for regulating AI (Talimonchik, [2021a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR292 "Talimonchik VP \(2021a\) The prospects for creating instruments for the coordination of activities of international organizations in the regulation of artificial intelligence. In: Osaba E, et al., \(eds\) Artificial intelligence: latest advances, new paradigms and novel applications. InTech Open, London")). Some proposed to make AI regulation and governance centralized within one organization to avoid having a fragmented landscape (Cihon et al., [2020a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR68 "Cihon P, Maas MM, Kemp L \(2020a\) Should artificial intelligence governance be centralised? Design lessons from history. AIES. 228–234. 
                  https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2001.03573
                  
                ")). This can take the form of a regulatory agency for AI (Erdélyi and Goldsmith, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR97 "Erdélyi OJ, Goldsmith J \(2022\) Regulating artificial intelligence proposal for a global solution. Gov Inf Q 39:101748. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.giq.2022.101748
                  
                ")), an International Artificial Intelligence Organization; an International Academy for AI Law and Regulation (Turner, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR304 "Turner J \(2019\) Robot rules: regulating artificial intelligence. Springer, Dordrecht")); or an international coordinating mechanism under the Group of 20 (Jelinek et al., [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR166 "Jelinek T, Wallach W, Kerimi D \(2021\) Policy brief: the creation of a G20 coordinating committee for the governance of artificial intelligence. AI Ethics 1:141–150. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43681-020-00019-y
                  
                ")). Meanwhile, others argue for a decentralized system where multiple fragmented institutions play a role stating that forum shopping is beneficial as these entities self-organize (Cihon et al., [2020b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR69 "Cihon P, Maas MM, Kemp L \(2020b\) Fragmentation and the future: Investigating architectures for international AI governance. Glob Policy 11:545556. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1758-5899.12890
                  
                ")).

Organizations such as the UN have the capacity to establish framework conventions including AI ones laying down general principles based on which more specific ones are adopted (Johnson and Bowman, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR167 "Johnson WG, Bowman DM \(2021\) A survey of instruments and institutions available for the global governance of artificial intelligence. IEEE Technol Soc Mag 40:68–76. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1109/MTS.2021.3123745
                  
                ")). Most recently, the UN adopted a resolution on seizing the opportunities of safe, secure, and trustworthy artificial intelligence systems for sustainable development. The resolution advocates for using AI to promote the 2030 agenda, supporting developing countries in its adoption, emphasizing the importance of domestic implementation through regulations, among many other points (United Nations General Assembly [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR376 "United Nations General Assembly \(2024\) Resolution 78/265. Seizing the opportunities of safe, secure and trustworthy artificial intelligence systems for sustainable development. A/RES/78/265")). The UN also adopted an interim report entitled: “Governing AI for Humanity” citing five guiding principles and seven institutional functions for the governance of AI (UN [2023a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR311 "United Nations \(2023a\) Interim report: governing AI for humanity. Published by advisory body on artificial intelligence. Accessed 20 November 2024")). This is occurring within the general framework of initiatives such as the 2024 Summit of the Future, the UN Common Agenda, and the Global Digital Compact. All these address global challenges, including AI governance (UN [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR313 "United Nations \(2024\) The summit of the future in 2024. 
                  https://www.un.org/en/common-agenda/summit-of-the-future
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024"); UN Common Agenda [n.d.a](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR384 "United Nations \(n.d.a\) Secretary-General’s report “Our Common Agenda”. 
                  https://www.un.org/en/content/common-agenda-report/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024").; UN [n.d.b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR383 "United Nations \(n.d.b\) Global digital compact. 
                  https://www.un.org/techenvoy/global-digital-compact
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Limitations concerning the work of such entities have been noticed mainly the lack of representation from lower-income countries; the absence of specificity in the AI instruments and initiatives established and lack of incentives for state compliance with norms (Johnson and Bowman, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR167 "Johnson WG, Bowman DM \(2021\) A survey of instruments and institutions available for the global governance of artificial intelligence. IEEE Technol Soc Mag 40:68–76. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1109/MTS.2021.3123745
                  
                ")). This affects the legitimacy of their decisions and initiatives in this case regarding AI (Chinen, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR64 "Chinen M \(2023\) The international governance of artificial intelligence. Edward Elgar Publishing, Cheltenham")). Yet, their leadership is very much needed (Fournier-Tombs, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR108 "Fournier-Tombs E \(2021\) Towards a United Nations internal regulation for artificial intelligence. Big Data Soc. 8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/20539517211039493
                  
                ")), as highlighted through their extensive focus on this topic (Giannini, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR124 "Giannini A \(2022\) Artificial intelligence, ethics, law: a view on the Italian and American debate \(and on their differences\). NJLP 51:248–263. 
                  https://doi.org/10.5553/NJLP/221307132022051002002
                  
                ")). Worth mentioning that these international organizations have already enlisted several NGOs and academic institutions while compiling the regulation drafts (recommendations, framework conventions and acts (United Nations General Assembly [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR376 "United Nations General Assembly \(2024\) Resolution 78/265. Seizing the opportunities of safe, secure and trustworthy artificial intelligence systems for sustainable development. A/RES/78/265"); UN Common Agenda n.da.; UN [n.d.b](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR383 "United Nations \(n.d.b\) Global digital compact. 
                  https://www.un.org/techenvoy/global-digital-compact
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")).

## AI regulatory authority

This section will examine the two main functions of a global AI organization: adopting international law and monitoring its enforcement domestically. It is worth mentioning that other functions will certainly be included and be subject to rigorous analysis.

### Establish law

AI entities are deemed to be operating within legal bounds if they are subject to human control. This should not be left to the discretion of states and corporations as they may prioritize their interests over other considerations (Burri, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR45 "Burri T \(2018\) International law and artificial intelligence. GYIL 60:91–108. 
                  https://doi.org/10.3790/gyil.60.1.91
                  
                ")). Moreover, confusion and uncertainty will rain if we use solutions on a case-by-case basis (Andrés, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR13 "Andrés MB \(2021\) Towards legal regulation of artificial intelligence. Rev IUS 15:35–53. 
                  https://doi.org/10.35487/rius.v15i48.2021.661
                  
                ")). Given that AI is a nascent field, the type of regulations to be adopted be it hard or soft law is witnessing a huge debate. Examples of nonbinding suggestions include the AI focus standards (Villasenor, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR330 "Villasenor J \(2020\) Soft law as a complement to AI regulation. Brookings. 
                  https://www.brookings.edu/articles/soft-law-as-a-complement-to-ai-regulation/
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")); principles for AI research and applications (Marchant, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR208 "Marchant G \(2019\) Soft law governance of artificial intelligence. UCLA: The Program on Understanding Law, Science, and Evidence \(PULSE\)")); voluntary safety commitments for AI (Han et al., [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR142 "Han TA, Lenaerts T, Santos FC, Pereira LM \(2022\) Voluntary safety commitments provide an escape from over-regulation in AI development. Technol Soc. 68. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.techsoc.2021.101843
                  
                ")); the EU Ethics Guidelines for Trustworthy AI, the Organization for Economic Co-Operation and Development Principles for responsible stewardship of trustworthy AI (OECD, [2019](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR377 "Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development \(2019\) Recommendation of the Council on artificial intelligence. 
                  https://legalinstruments.oecd.org/api/print?ids=648&lang=en
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")); UN Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization Recommendations on the Ethics of Artificial Intelligence (UNESCO [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR369 "United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization \(2021\) Recommendation on the ethics of artificial intelligence. 
                  https://www.unesco.org/en/articles/recommendation-ethics-artificial-intelligence?
                  
                ")). Hard law proposals cover the calls for UN to establish a regulatory framework for AI (Fournier-Tombs ([2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR108 "Fournier-Tombs E \(2021\) Towards a United Nations internal regulation for artificial intelligence. Big Data Soc. 8. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1177/20539517211039493
                  
                ")) and the use of global commons as a structure (Tzimas, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR305 "Tzimas T \(2018\) Artificial intelligence as global commons and the “international law supremacy” principle. In: Striełkowski W, et al., \(eds\) Advances in social science, education and humanities research. Atlantis. Press, Dordrecht")). Meanwhile, some call for considering the EU AI Act as global AI law (Musch et al., [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR227 "Musch S, Borrelli M, Kerrigan C \(2023\) The EU AI act as global artificial intelligence regulation")), while similar proposals are expected to occur with regards to the Council of Europe Framework Convention on Artificial Intelligence (Council of Europe, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR74 "Council of Europe \(2024\) Framework Convention on Artificial Intelligence, Human Rights, Democracy and the Rule of Law")). Many stakeholders are making suggestions such as the G20; standards organizations, NGOs and research institutes (Schmitt, [2022](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR268 "Schmitt L \(2022\) Mapping global AI governance: a nascent regime in a fragmented landscape. AI Ethics 2:303–314. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s43681-021-00083-y
                  
                ")).

A push towards a soft approach is noticed due to various reasons. These are the need for flexibility due to the uncertainties surrounding this field; states reluctance to relinquish their sovereignty and control of information (Erdélyi and Goldsmith, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR96 "Erdélyi OJ, Goldsmith J \(2018\) Regulating artificial intelligence proposal for a global solution. 2018 AAAI/ACM Conference on AI, Ethics, and Society \(AIES ’18\), February 2–3, 2018, New Orleans, LA, USA")); the fast development of this technology cannot be matched through rules (Marchant, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR209 "Marchant G \(2021\) Why soft law is the best way to approach the pacing problem in AI. Carnegie Council for Ethics in International Affairs. 
                  https://www.carnegiecouncil.org/media/article/why-soft-law-is-the-best-way-to-approach-the-pacing-problem-in-ai
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")); geopolitical and economic competition as well as national security (Whyman, [2023](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR343 "Whyman B \(2023\) AI regulation is coming—what is the likely outcome? Center for Strategic & International Studies. 
                  https://www.csis.org/blogs/strategic-technologies-blog/ai-regulation-coming-what-likely-outcome
                  
                . Accessed 20 November 2024")). Yet, as seen earlier, two binding instruments are already adopted (EU AI Act, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR364 "EU AI Act \(2024\) European Parliament legislative resolution of 13 March 2024 on the proposal for a regulation of the European Parliament and of the Council on laying down harmonised rules on Artificial Intelligence \(Artificial Intelligence Act\) and amending certain Union Legislative Acts \(COM\(2021\)0206 – C9-0146/2021 – 2021/0106\(COD\)\)"); Council of Europe, [2024](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR74 "Council of Europe \(2024\) Framework Convention on Artificial Intelligence, Human Rights, Democracy and the Rule of Law")). Therefore, a global AI authority should start by using nonbinding instruments such as recommendations, guidelines, and standards (Erdélyi and Goldsmith, [2018](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR96 "Erdélyi OJ, Goldsmith J \(2018\) Regulating artificial intelligence proposal for a global solution. 2018 AAAI/ACM Conference on AI, Ethics, and Society \(AIES ’18\), February 2–3, 2018, New Orleans, LA, USA")), while considering the provisions established within AI treaties. This responds to governments need to balance innovation with protection (Smuha, [2021](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR276 "Smuha NA \(2021\) From a ‘race to AI’ to a ‘race to AI regulation’: regulatory competition for artificial intelligence. Law Innov Technol 13:57–84. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/17579961.2021.1898300
                  
                ")) as AI covers numerous technologies across a variety of sectors (Schiff, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR267 "Schiff D \(2020\) What’s next for AI ethics, policy, and governance? A global overview. In Proceedings of the AAAI/ACM conference on AI, ethics, and society 20:153–158. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1145/3375627.3375804
                  
                ")). Elements to be considered by the future regulatory authority include self-determination; transparency; discrimination and rule of law (Wishmeyer and Rademacher, [2020](/articles/s41599-024-03560-x#ref-CR348 "Wischmeyer T, Rademacher T \(2020\) Regulating artificial intelligence. Springer, Dordrecht")).
